The Definition of Qiyas and Its Five Close Forms
Defining qiyas and enumerating its five close forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qiyas as a composite statement that entails its conclusion 'by itself' (lizatihi) and identify its five close forms, distinguishing them from induction and example.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a syllogism whose conclusion follows necessarily from its premises alone.

In this passage, the commentator explains the precise definition of qiyas (analogical reasoning) as understood in Hanafi usul. He emphasizes that qiyas must be 'lizatihi' — its conclusion must follow from the premises without needing an additional premise. This restriction excludes induction (istiqra') and example (tamthil) from the category of valid legal evidence. The text then enumerates the five 'close forms' (suwar qaribah) of qiyas, which are the only syllogistic structures that produce immediate necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Locate the definition of qiyas in the passage: it is a composite statement of premises that entail another statement by themselves (lizatihi). This excludes any inference that requires an external premise.
Note that the restriction 'lizatihi' is crucial; it is what distinguishes qiyas from induction and example.
2
Understand the exclusion of induction and example
The passage explicitly states that the purpose of the 'lizatihi' condition is to confine evidence to qiyas and to exclude induction and example. Induction and example do not produce necessary conclusions by themselves.
Remember: Induction enumerates particulars but does not guarantee the universal, and example relies on similarity rather than strict entailment.
3
Recognize the five close forms as immediate
The matn states that qiyas has five close forms. 'Close' means the conclusion follows immediately from the premises without a foreign premise. The commentary explains that the author limited the discussion to these five for brevity, refuting usul scholars who discussed many more forms.
These five forms include three from categorical iqtirani and two from istithna'i (conditional) syllogisms.
4
Analyze the first form (first figure)
The first form is the first figure of categorical syllogism: the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major. Conditions: minor must be affirmative (ijab al-sughra) and major universal (kulliyat al-kubra). The passage provides examples (e.g., 'Every horse is an animal, every animal is sentient, so every horse is sentient').
The text criticizes al-Tahrir for suggesting that when the two ends of the major are equal, the affirmation of the minor is unnecessary. Such a syllogism is not productive 'by itself'.
5
Address the objection about negative syllogisms
An objection is raised: 'A is not B, and everything not B is C.' The answer: Negation is pure removal, but the major contains an implicit affirmative (the predicate 'not B' still has existential consideration). If you consider that existence in the minor, then the minor becomes affirmative with a negative predicate; otherwise there is no subsumption.
This shows that even negative-appearing premises rely on an underlying affirmation for the syllogism to be valid 'lizatihi'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اخراج کے لئے لائی گئی ہے جن میں لزوم مقدمہ اجنبہ کے واسطہ سے ہوتا ہے مگر یہ بات پوشیدہ نہ رہے کہ تاسیس تاکید سے زیادہ بہتر ہے۔ حاصل یہ کہ لذاتہ کی قید خواہ تاکیدی ہو یا تاسیسی اس سے مقصود تعریف دلیل کو قیاس میں محدود و منحصر کرنا اور استقراء تمثیل کو دلیل سے خارج کرنا ہے اور اس امر کو واضح کرنا ہے کہ قیاس وہ قول ہے جو مرکب ہو ایسے قضایا سے جو لذا نتہ قول آخر گوستلزم ہو۔
جیے ان کان زیدانساناً كان حيوانا لكنه انسان ينتج فهو حيوان وان كان زيد حمارا كان ناهقا لكنه ليس اهق ينتج انه ليس بحمار یا زید انسان و کل انسان حيوان ينتج زيد حيوان
متن المسلم :- وله خمس صور قريبة الاولى ان يعلم حكم لكل افراد شئى ثم يعلم بثوته للآخر كلا او بعضاً فيلزم ثبوت ذالك الحكم للآخر کذالک بالضرورة فلابد من ايجاب الصغرى وكلية الكبرى ومافى التحرير في طرفى الكبرى فليس بشئى لانه ليس لذاته وأورد أليس ب وكل ماليس ب ج والجواب أن السلب من حيث هو هو رفع محض وعقد الوضع فى الكبرى لا يخلو من ملاحظة ثبوت فان لاحظته في الصغرى فلاسلب بل ايجاب سلب والافلا اندراج :
ترجمہ : ۔ اور قیاس کی پانچ قریبی صورتیں ہیں پہلی صورت یہ ہے کہ کسی شئی کے ہر ہر فرد کا حکم معلوم کر لیا جائے پھر اس شئی کا ثبوت دوسری شئی کے لئے کل یا بعضاً معلوم ہو جائے پس اسطرح اس حکم کا ثبوت دوسرے کے لئے ایسا ہی بالضرورة لازم ہوگا اس لئے اس میں ایجاب صغریٰ اور کلیت کبری کی شرط ضروری ہے۔ اور وہ جو تحریر میں ہے کہ کبری کے دونوں طرف مساوی ہونے کی صورت میں( ایجاب صغری کی شرط ضروری نہیں ) تو وہ بلاوجہ کی بات ہے۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں منتج ہونا لذا تہ نہیں ہے اور اکیس ب و کل مالیس ب ج سے اعتراض کیا گیا جواب یہ ہے کہ سب بحیثیت سلب رفع محض کا نام ہے اور کبری میں عقد وضع لحاظ ثبوت سے خالی نہیں لہذا اس ثبوت کا لحاظ اگر صغری میں کر لیا جائے تو صغری سالبہ نہیں رہ جائے گا بلکہ موجب سالبتہ الحمول ہو جائے گا اور اگر صغریٰ میں اس ثبوت کا لحاظ نہ کیا جائے تو مندرج نہیں ہوگا۔
تشریح : دلیل کی مؤخر الذکر تعریف کرنے کے بعد مصنف نے کہا تھا کہ یہ تعریف قیاس کے ساتھ مختص ہے اس لئے اب یہاں قیاس کی ان صورتوں کو بیان کر رہے جسکا بذات خود بغیر کسی مقدمہ اجتنبیہ کے واسطے کے نتیجہ دینا ظاہر ہے پس کہا وہ شمس صور قریبہ الخ۔
قوله " وله خمس صورقريبة“ یہ بات کو معلوم ہے کہ قیاس کی دو قسمیں ہیں استثنائی واقترانی ۔ پھر اقترانی کی دو قسمیں ہوتی ہیں عملی شرطی، اور اقترانی شرطی میں انتاج غیر بین ہوتا ہے کہ بعض میں مقدمہ غریبہ کی حاجت ہوتی ہے البتہ اقترانی حملی کا نتیجہ بین ہوتا ہے تاہم اس کی بعض صورتیں جیسے شکل کا نتیجہ دینا ذہن سے بعید ہے اس لئے ماتن نے اقترانی حملی کی بعض صورتوں اور اقترانی شرطی کی بعض صورتوں کے ذکر سے مکمل صرف نظر کیا اور بیان کو اقترانی حملی کی تین صورتوں اور استثنائی کی دو وجھوں پر منحصر و محدود کر دیا اس لئے کہا وہ شمس صور قریبہ ۔
یعنی قیاس کی کل پانچ صورتیں صرف ایسی ہیں جو عند العقل انتاج کے لحاظ سے ایک دوسرے سے قریب ہیں صور قیاس کو صرف پانچ پر منحصر کر کے مصنف نے گویا رد کیا ہے علامہ ابن حاجب اور امام ابن ہمام وغیر ہما علماء اصول کا جنہوں نے اس مقام پر قیاس کے جملہ صور قریبہ وبعیدہ اور استقراء کے احوال کو بیان کر کے کلام کو طول دیا ہے حالانکہ ذکر مبادی میں تطویل لا طائل کلام کا ذکر کرنا فضول ہے کیونکہ اس سے غرض متعلق نہیں ہو مباری میں صرف اس قدر کلام ذکر کرنا چاہئے جو ضروری اور اہم ہو اسی لئے ماتن نے صرف پانچ صور قریبہ کو ذکر کیا۔
قوله الاولى ان يعلم حكم نكل افراد شئى الخ درج بالا عبارت سے مصنف نے قیاس کی پہلی صورت کو بیان کیا ہے اس کی تفصیل یہ ہے۔ صورت اولیٰ : شکل اول کی ہے جس میں حد وسط صغریٰ میں محمول اور کبری میں موضوع ہوتا ہے اور اس کی شرط یہ ہے کہ صغری موجبہ ہو اور کبری کلیہ ہو، اس بنا پر یہ شکل مندرجہ ذیل چار ضروب میں نتیجہ دیتی ہے۔ (۱) ضروب اول: صغری موجہ کلیہ، کبری موجبہ کلیہ سے مرکب ہو جیسے کل فرس حیوان وکل حیوان حساس، فكل فرس حساس (۲) ضرب دوم: صغری موجبہ کلیہ اور کبری سالبہ کلیہ سے مرکب ہو ، جیسے کل حمار حیوان
It is brought for extraction, in which the entailment occurs through the intermediary of a foreign premise. But it should not remain hidden that establishing is better than confirming. The outcome: the restriction 'by itself' whether confirmatory or establishing, its purpose is to confine and limit the definition of evidence to qiyas, and to exclude induction and example from evidence, and to clarify that qiyas is a statement composed of propositions that by themselves entail another statement. For example: If Zayd is a human, then he is an animal; but he is a human, so it results that he is an animal. And if Zayd is a donkey, then he brays; but he does not bray, so it results that he is not a donkey. Or: Zayd is human, and every human is an animal; so it results that Zayd is an animal.
Matn of al-Muslim: 'And it has five close forms: The first: that the ruling of every individual of a thing is known, then its being established for another, wholly or partially, is known; so the establishment of that ruling for the other is necessary in the same way necessarily. Therefore, the affirmation of the minor premise and the universality of the major premise are necessary. And what is in al-Tahrir regarding the two ends of the major being equal is nothing, because it is not by itself. And it was objected: 'A is not B, and everything not B is C?' The answer: Negation as such is pure removal, and the affirmative judgment in the major cannot be devoid of consideration of existence. If you consider it in the minor, then it is not a negative but an affirmative of a negative predicate; otherwise there is no subsumption.'
Translation: And qiyas has five close forms. The first form: that the ruling of every individual of a thing is known, then the establishment of that thing for another, wholly or partially, becomes known. So in this way, the establishment of that ruling for the other is necessarily incumbent in the same way. Therefore, the conditions of affirmation of the minor and universality of the major are necessary. And what is in al-Tahrir that in the case of the two ends of the major being equal (the condition of affirmation of the minor is not necessary) is baseless. Because in that case, being productive is not by itself. And an objection was posed: 'A is not B, and everything not B is C.' The answer: Negation as such is pure removal, and the affirmative judgment in the major is not free from consideration of existence. So if this existence is considered in the minor, then the minor will not remain negative, but rather it becomes an affirmative with a negative predicate; and if this existence is not considered in the minor, then it will not be subsumed.
Elaboration: After defining evidence with the later definition, the author said that this definition is specific to qiyas. Therefore, here he is explaining those forms of qiyas whose giving a result by themselves without any foreign premise is apparent. So he said: 'And it has five close forms, etc.'
His statement: 'And it has five close forms' – it is known that qiyas is of two types: istithna'i and iqtirani. Then iqtirani is of two types: conjunctive conditional, and the productiveness of conditional iqtirani is not apparent, as in some cases a foreign premise is needed. However, the result of categorical iqtirani is apparent, but some of its forms, like producing the conclusion of a figure, are far from the mind. Therefore, the matn author completely refrained from mentioning some forms of categorical iqtirani and some forms of conditional iqtirani, and confined the discussion to three forms of categorical iqtirani and two forms of istithna'i. That is why he said: 'five close forms.'
That is, the total of five forms of qiyas are only those that are close to each other in terms of productivity according to reason. By limiting the forms of qiyas to only five, the author has implicitly refuted 'Allamah Ibn al-Hajib and Imam Ibn al-Humam and other usul scholars who, in this context, mentioned all the close and distant forms of qiyas and the states of induction, lengthening the discourse, whereas in the discussion of preliminaries, such unnecessary lengthening is futile because the purpose is not related to it. In the preliminaries, only that much discussion should be mentioned which is necessary and important. That is why the matn author mentioned only five close forms.
His statement: 'The first: that the ruling of every individual of a thing is known, etc.' With the above phrase, the author has described the first form of qiyas. Its detail: The first form: it is of the first figure, in which the middle term is the predicate in the minor and the subject in the major. Its condition: the minor must be affirmative and the major universal. On this basis, this figure yields a conclusion in the following four moods: (1) First mood: composed of an affirmative universal minor and an affirmative universal major, e.g., Every horse is an animal, and every animal is sentient, so every horse is sentient. (2) Second mood: composed of an affirmative universal minor and a negative universal major, e.g., Every donkey is an animal...
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of qiyas and its classification. It begins by clarifying that the phrase 'by itself' in the definition serves to exclude induction and example from the scope of evidence (dalil). Qiyas is defined as a composite statement (qawl murakkab) of premises that necessarily entail another statement by themselves, without needing an external premise. The author then presents the matn text stating that qiyas has five 'close forms' (suwar qaribah), meaning those forms whose conclusion follows immediately without additional premises. The first form is explained: when the ruling of every individual of a thing (e.g., all men are mortal) is known, and then it is known that this thing applies to another (e.g., Zayd is a man), the ruling applies to that other necessarily. Conditions: the minor must be affirmative (ijab al-sughra) and the major universal (kulliyat al-kubra). The text criticizes al-Tahrir for suggesting that when both ends of the major are equal, affirmation of the minor is unnecessary—such a syllogism is not productive 'by itself'. It then addresses an objection regarding negative syllogisms: if someone says 'A is not B, and everything not B is C', the answer is that negation is mere removal, and the major contains an implicit affirmative (the predicate 'not B' still has an existential consideration). If this existence is considered in the minor, the minor becomes affirmative with a negative predicate; otherwise the minor does not fall under the major. The commentary then explains the author's rationale for listing only five forms: qiyas is of two kinds (istithna'i and iqtirani), and iqtirani has categorical and conditional subtypes. The conditional iqtirani often needs a foreign premise, so only the categorical iqtirani forms that yield immediate results are mentioned, along with two from istithna'i. This brevity is intentional, contrasting with Ibn al-Hajib and Ibn al-Humam who discussed many forms. Finally, the first form is detailed as the first figure of categorical syllogism, with the middle term as predicate in the minor and subject in the major, requiring an affirmative minor and universal major. Four moods are listed; the first two are given as examples: affirmative-affirmative and affirmative-negative.

Key Terms
لذاته (lidhātihi): The property of a syllogism that its conclusion follows from the premises alone without any added premise, distinguishing it from induction and example.
صور قريبة (ṣuwar qarībah): Forms of qiyas whose conclusion follows immediately from the premises, without the need for a foreign premise to make the entailment apparent.
إيجاب الصغرى (ījāb al-ṣughrā): Condition that the minor premise must be affirmative (not negative) for a valid first figure syllogism.
كلية الكبرى (kulliyyat al-kubrā): Condition that the major premise must be universal (quantified over all individuals) for a valid syllogism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the minor premise be affirmative in the first figure of qiyas?
A) To ensure the syllogism is in Arabic
B) To allow the middle term to be predicate in the minor and subject in the major
C) Because a negative minor would require an additional premise for subsumption
D) Because the major premise must be universal

Key Takeaway
The definition of qiyas requires that the conclusion follow necessarily from the premises alone (lizatihi); only the five close forms, with an affirmative minor and universal major in the first figure, meet this condition and are valid for istinbat.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
The Five Proximate Forms of Qiyas and Their Rationale
Classification of qiyas forms and rationale for restriction to five

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main types of qiyas (istithna'i and iqtirani) and explain why Hanafi usul restricts productive qiyas to only five proximate forms.

Core Idea
Qiyas has only five proximate forms that yield conclusions obviously; others require extra premises.

In this passage, the commentator explains why the author of the matn limited qiyas to five 'proximate' forms. Understanding this classification is crucial for recognizing which logical structures are self-sufficient in legal reasoning and which require additional premises. The commentator also critiques earlier scholars for overextending the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types of qiyas
Recognize that qiyas is divided into exceptional (istithna'i) and conjunctive (iqtirani). Conjunctive further divides into categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti).
Hanafi usul treats these as fundamental divisions.
2
Understand why only five forms are 'proximate'
Conditional conjunctive often needs an extra premise for conclusion, so it is not proximate. Even in categorical conjunctive, some forms (like remote figures) are not obvious. The author omits these and retains only three categorical and two exceptional forms that produce conclusions without external aid.
This is a pedagogical choice to avoid unnecessary length.
3
Analyze the implicit critique of other scholars
The commentator states that by restricting to five forms, the matn author implicitly refutes Ibn Hajib, Ibn al-Humam, and others who discussed all forms including remote ones and induction topics.
This highlights the Hanafi focus on practical utility in usul works.
4
Connect to the broader purpose of qiyas in fiqh
The proximate forms are those that reliably produce a legal ruling (zanni) from premises without external premises. This is the backbone of analogical reasoning in Hanafi jurisprudence.
The conditions for validity are detailed in the next lesson.

(۳) ضرب سوم :- صغری موجبہ جزئیہ و کبری موجبہ کلیہ سے مرکب ہو ۔ جیسے بعض الحیوان انسان وكل انسان ناطق، فبعض الحيوان ناطق
(۴) ضرب چہارم : ۔ صغری موجہ جزئیہ اور کبری سالیہ کلیہ سے مرکب ہو جیسے بعض الحيوان ساسد لاشئى من الاسد بصاهل فبعض الحيوان ليس بصاهل
حاصل عبارت مصنف : مصنف کے الفاظ میں صورت اولی کے بیان کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ پہلے کسی شئی (اوسط) کے ہر فرد کا حکم ایجابی یا سلیبی جانا جائے ( مثلا حیوان کے ہر فرد کے بارے میں معلوم ہے کہ وہ ماشئی ہے ) پھر وہ شئی (اوسط) دوسرے کے لئے (اکبر ) کلایا بعضا یعنی اس کے ہر فرد کے لئے یا لبعض افراد کے لئے ثابت ہو جیسے حیوان ہونا انسان کے ہر فرد کے لئے ثابت ہے ) تو اکبر بھی اصغر کے ہر فرد یا بعض افراد کے لئے ایجابا یا سلباً ثابت ہوگا جیسا کہ مثال مذکور میں ماشئی ہونا انسان کے ہر فرد کے لئے ثابت ہو رہا ہے کسی موضوع کے جملہ افراد کے لئے ایک حکم (ایجابی و سلبی ) معلوم کیا جائے مثال کے طور پر لفظ انسان قضیہ میں موضوع واقع ہو تو اس کے جملہ افراد کے لئے حکم حیوانیت ثابت مان کر یوں کہا جائے کل انسان حیوان ۔ یا پھر اسی لفظ انسان جو قضیہ کا موضوع ہے کے جمیع افراد سے سلب حجریت کر کے یہ کہا جائے لاشئ من الانسان بحجر ، یہ قیاس کے مقدمہ ثانیہ، کبری کا حاصل ہے۔ بعدہ یہ معلوم کیا جائے گا کہ یہی موضوع دوسری چیز کے لئے کلا یا بعضاً ثابت ہے جیسے ہی انسان کو، کاتب کے کل افراد کے لئے ثابت کر کے اس طرح کہیں کل کا تب انسان ، یا جسم کے بعض افراد کے لئے انسانیت کا علم لگا کر یہ کہیں بعض الجسم انسان، تو اسطرح قیاس کے مقدمہ اولی صغری کا حاصل نکل آتا ہے۔ اور پھر صغری، کبری کو ترتیب دینے سے ایک علم حاصل ہو جاتا ہے جو پہلے حاصل نہ تھا" چنانچہ مثال کل کا تب انسان و کل انسان حیوان کی ترتیب سے معلوم ہوا کہ ہر کا تب حیوان ہے یونہی کل کا تب انسان ولاشئ من الانسان بجر“ سے معلوم ہوا کہ کا تب کا کوئی فرد حجر نہیں ہے۔ اسی طرح بعض الجسم انسان و کل انسان حیوان کی ترتیب سے معلوم ہوا کہ بعض جسم حیوان ہے۔ غرض اس توضیح وتمثیل سے ظاہر ہے کہ قیاس کی اس صورت میں صغری کا موجبہ ہونا اور کبری کا کلیہ ہونا شرط ہے
Explanation: After defining the evidence (dalil) in the latter part, the author said that this definition is specific to qiyas (analogy). So now he mentions the forms of qiyas that by themselves, without any external premise, produce a conclusion that is apparent. Thus he said: "and it has five proximate forms" etc. His statement: "and it has five proximate forms" — it is known that qiyas has two types: exceptional (istithna'i) and conjunctive (iqtirani). Then conjunctive has two types: categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti). In conditional conjunctive, the productivity (intaj) is not apparent because sometimes it needs a strange premise. However, the result of categorical conjunctive is apparent, yet some of its forms, like the conclusion of the first figure, are remote from the mind. Therefore, the author (al-Matn) completely omitted mentioning some forms of categorical conjunctive and some forms of conditional conjunctive, and restricted the exposition to three forms of categorical conjunctive and two forms of exceptional (istithna'i). Thus he said: "five proximate forms". That is, there are only five forms of qiyas that are, for the intellect, close to each other in terms of productivity. By restricting the forms of qiyas to only five, the author implicitly refutes 'Allamah Ibn Hajib and Imam Ibn al-Humam and other usul scholars who, at this point, mentioned all proximate and remote forms of qiyas and the conditions of induction (istiqra'), lengthening the discourse, whereas in mentioning preliminaries, extending talk without benefit is wasteful because the purpose is not served. In such contexts, only that much talk should be mentioned which is necessary and important. Therefore, al-Matn mentioned only the five proximate forms. His statement: "The first is that the ruling of each individual of something is known" etc. — From the above phrase, the author has described the first form of qiyas. Its detail is as follows: The first form is of the first figure (shakl awwal), in which the middle term is the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise. Its condition is that the minor premise be affirmative and the major premise be universal. On this basis, this figure yields a conclusion in the following four moods (darb): (1) First mood: Composed of an affirmative universal minor and an affirmative universal major, e.g., 'Every horse is an animal, and every animal is sentient, so every horse is sentient.' (2) Second mood: Composed of an affirmative universal minor and a negative universal major, e.g., 'Every donkey is an animal, and nothing from the animal is stone, so nothing from the donkey is stone.' (3) Third mood: Composed of an affirmative particular minor and an affirmative universal major, e.g., 'Some animals are human, and every human is rational, so some animals are rational.' (4) Fourth mood: Composed of an affirmative particular minor and a negative universal major, e.g., 'Some animals are lions, and nothing from the lion is neighing, so some animals are not neighing.' The gist of the author's phrase: The summary of the explanation of the first form in the author's words is that: First, the affirmative or negative ruling of each individual of a thing (the middle term) is known (e.g., it is known about every individual of animal that it is sentient). Then that thing (middle term) is proven for another thing (the major term) either universally or partially (i.e., it is proven for every individual of it or for some individuals, like being animal is proven for every individual of human). So the major term will also be proven affirmatively or negatively for every individual or some individuals of the minor term, as in the mentioned example, being sentient is proven for every individual of human. For all individuals of a subject, a ruling (affirmative or negative) is known. For example, if the word 'human' occurs as the subject in a proposition, then the ruling of 'animality' is considered proven for all its individuals, so it is said: 'Every human is an animal.' Or, stripping the ruling of 'stoneness' from all individuals of the same subject 'human', it is said: 'Nothing from human is stone.' This is the result of the second premise of qiyas, the major premise. After that, it will be determined that this same subject is proven for another thing universally or partially, e.g., proving 'human' for every individual of 'writer' so it is said: 'Every writer is human,' or attaching the knowledge of 'humanity' to some individuals of 'body' so it is said: 'Some bodies are human.' Thus the result of the first premise of qiyas, the minor premise, comes out. Then from combining the minor and major premises, a knowledge is obtained that was not previously had. Indeed, from combining 'Every writer is an animal' and 'Every animal is sentient', it is known that every writer is sentient. Similarly, from 'Every writer is human' and 'Nothing from human is stone', it is known that no individual of writer is stone. Likewise, from 'Some body is human' and 'Every human is animal', it is known that some body is animal. In short, from this clarification and exemplification, it is evident that in this form of qiyas, it is a condition that the minor premise be affirmative and the major premise be universal.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Nu'ut, a commentary on Muslim al-Thubut, focuses on the classification of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by explaining why the text restricts qiyas to only five 'proximate forms' (صور قريبة). He notes that qiyas is of two main types: exceptional (istithna'i) and conjunctive (iqtirani). Conjunctive further divides into categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti). Conditional conjunctive often requires an additional premise to produce a conclusion, so it is not 'proximate'. Even in categorical conjunctive, some forms (like the first figure) produce conclusions that are not immediately obvious to the mind. Therefore, the matn author deliberately omits many forms and focuses only on three forms of categorical conjunctive and two of exceptional — totalling five 'proximate' forms that naturally yield conclusions without external premises. This is a methodological choice to avoid unnecessary lengthening, implicitly critiquing earlier scholars like Ibn Hajib and Ibn al-Humam for delving into all forms including remote ones and conditions of induction. The passage then details the first form, which corresponds to the first figure of Aristotelian logic. In this figure, the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major premises. The condition for a valid conclusion is that the minor must be affirmative and the major universal. This yields four valid moods: (1) affirmative universal minor, affirmative universal major (Barbara); (2) affirmative universal minor, negative universal major (Celarent); (3) affirmative particular minor, affirmative universal major (Darii); (4) affirmative particular minor, negative universal major (Ferio). Examples are given for each, using legal-like terms (animal, human, stone, etc.) to illustrate how a known ruling is extended. In the final summary, the author explains the underlying logic: first identify the middle term's ruling (e.g., every animal is sentient); then establish that the middle term applies to the minor term (e.g., every writer is human); then the major term's ruling transfers to the minor. This is essentially the mechanism of categorical syllogism. The commentator emphasizes the conditions: minor affirmative (because a negative minor would not connect the middle to the minor) and major universal (to ensure the conclusion applies to all or some of the minor). This discussion is foundational for Hanafi reasoning in deriving rulings by qiyas, where the validity of an analogy depends on clear and necessary logical connections.

Key Terms
استثنائي (istithna'i): A type of qiyas where the conclusion is drawn from a conditional premise and its antecedent or consequent
اقتراني (iqtirani): A type of qiyas composed of two premises (minor and major) without a conditional
حملي (hamli): A conjunctive syllogism where both premises are categorical propositions (subject-predicate)
شكل أول (shakl awwal): The first figure of syllogism where the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major
ضرب (darb): A mood or combination of quantity and quality of premises in a given figure
إنتاج (intaj): The act of deriving a conclusion from premises

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Hanafi usul limit valid qiyas to five proximate forms?
A) Other forms are logically invalid.
B) Other forms require additional premises or produce conclusions that are not immediately obvious.
C) The matn author was unaware of other forms.
D) Only these five forms are mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul recognizes only five proximate forms of qiyas that yield conclusions without external premises, prioritising clarity and practicality.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
10 min
The First Form of Qiyas: Conditions and Four Valid Moods
Detailed explanation of the first form of qiyas (first figure) with conditions and four moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the first figure of qiyas and enumerate its four valid moods with examples.

Core Idea
In the first figure, the minor must be affirmative and the major universal; this yields four moods.

After establishing the five proximate forms, the commentator delves into the first form, which corresponds to the first figure of Aristotelian logic. This figure is foundational for qiyas in Hanafi usul. Understanding its conditions and moods is essential for constructing sound analogies in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define the first figure's structure
The middle term is the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise.
This structure ensures the middle term connects the two premises.
2
Identify the conditions for validity
The minor premise must be affirmative, and the major premise must be universal.
A negative minor would break the connection; a particular major would not distribute the middle.
3
Enumerate the four valid moods
List the four combinations of minor/major quality and quantity: affirmative universal & affirmative universal; affirmative universal & negative universal; affirmative particular & affirmative universal; affirmative particular & negative universal.
These are the only moods that satisfy the conditions.
4
Work through an example for each mood
For each mood, identify the minor, major, and conclusion, and verify that the conditions are met.
The passage provides examples with animals, humans, etc.
5
Connect to legal qiyas
In fiqh, the minor premise represents the new case (far') having the illa, and the major premise represents the original ruling (asl) with the illa.
This logical structure underlies all valid analogies.

Source Text

تشریح : دلیل کی مؤخر الذکر تعریف کرنے کے بعد مصنف نے کہا تھا کہ یہ تعریف قیاس کے ساتھ مختص ہے اس لئے اب یہاں قیاس کی ان صورتوں کو بیان کر رہے جسکا بذات خود بغیر کسی مقدمہ اجتنبیہ کے واسطے کے نتیجہ دینا ظاہر ہے پس کہا وہ شمس صور قریبہ الخ۔
قوله " وله خمس صورقريبة“ یہ بات کو معلوم ہے کہ قیاس کی دو قسمیں ہیں استثنائی واقترانی ۔ پھر اقترانی کی دو قسمیں ہوتی ہیں عملی شرطی، اور اقترانی شرطی میں انتاج غیر بین ہوتا ہے کہ بعض میں مقدمہ غریبہ کی حاجت ہوتی ہے البتہ اقترانی حملی کا نتیجہ بین ہوتا ہے تاہم اس کی بعض صورتیں جیسے شکل کا نتیجہ دینا ذہن سے بعید ہے اس لئے ماتن نے اقترانی حملی کی بعض صورتوں اور اقترانی شرطی کی بعض صورتوں کے ذکر سے مکمل صرف نظر کیا اور بیان کو اقترانی حملی کی تین صورتوں اور استثنائی کی دو وجھوں پر منحصر و محدود کر دیا اس لئے کہا وہ شمس صور قریبہ ۔
یعنی قیاس کی کل پانچ صورتیں صرف ایسی ہیں جو عند العقل انتاج کے لحاظ سے ایک دوسرے سے قریب ہیں صور قیاس کو صرف پانچ پر منحصر کر کے مصنف نے گویا رد کیا ہے علامہ ابن حاجب اور امام ابن ہمام وغیر ہما علماء اصول کا جنہوں نے اس مقام پر قیاس کے جملہ صور قریبہ وبعیدہ اور استقراء کے احوال کو بیان کر کے کلام کو طول دیا ہے حالانکہ ذکر مبادی میں تطویل لا طائل کلام کا ذکر کرنا فضول ہے کیونکہ اس سے غرض متعلق نہیں ہو مباری میں صرف اس قدر کلام ذکر کرنا چاہئے جو ضروری اور اہم ہو اسی لئے ماتن نے صرف پانچ صور قریبہ کو ذکر کیا۔
قوله الاولى ان يعلم حكم نكل افراد شئى الخ درج بالا عبارت سے مصنف نے قیاس کی پہلی صورت کو بیان کیا ہے اس کی تفصیل یہ ہے۔ صورت اولیٰ : شکل اول کی ہے جس میں حد وسط صغریٰ میں محمول اور کبری میں موضوع ہوتا ہے اور اس کی شرط یہ ہے کہ صغری موجبہ ہو اور کبری کلیہ ہو، اس بنا پر یہ شکل مندرجہ ذیل چار ضروب میں نتیجہ دیتی ہے۔ (۱) ضروب اول: صغری موجہ کلیہ، کبری موجبہ کلیہ سے مرکب ہو جیسے کل فرس حیوان وکل حیوان حساس، فكل فرس حساس (۲) ضرب دوم: صغری موجبہ کلیہ اور کبری سالبہ کلیہ سے مرکب ہو ، جیسے کل حمار حیوان ولاشئى من الحيوان بحجر فلاشئى من الحمار بحجر -
(۳) ضرب سوم :- صغری موجبہ جزئیہ و کبری موجبہ کلیہ سے مرکب ہو ۔ جیسے بعض الحیوان انسان وكل انسان ناطق، فبعض الحيوان ناطق

On this basis, this figure yields a conclusion in the following four moods (darb): (1) First mood: Composed of an affirmative universal minor and an affirmative universal major, e.g., 'Every horse is an animal, and every animal is sentient, so every horse is sentient.' (2) Second mood: Composed of an affirmative universal minor and a negative universal major, e.g., 'Every donkey is an animal, and nothing from the animal is stone, so nothing from the donkey is stone.' (3) Third mood: Composed of an affirmative particular minor and an affirmative universal major, e.g., 'Some animals are human, and every human is rational, so some animals are rational.' (4) Fourth mood: Composed of an affirmative particular minor and a negative universal major, e.g., 'Some animals are lions, and nothing from the lion is neighing, so some animals are not neighing.' The gist of the author's phrase: The summary of the explanation of the first form in the author's words is that: First, the affirmative or negative ruling of each individual of a thing (the middle term) is known (e.g., it is known about every individual of animal that it is sentient). Then that thing (middle term) is proven for another thing (the major term) either universally or partially (i.e., it is proven for every individual of it or for some individuals, like being animal is proven for every individual of human). So the major term will also be proven affirmatively or negatively for every individual or some individuals of the minor term, as in the mentioned example, being sentient is proven for every individual of human. For all individuals of a subject, a ruling (affirmative or negative) is known. For example, if the word 'human' occurs as the subject in a proposition, then the ruling of 'animality' is considered proven for all its individuals, so it is said: 'Every human is an animal.' Or, stripping the ruling of 'stoneness' from all individuals of the same subject 'human', it is said: 'Nothing from human is stone.' This is the result of the second premise of qiyas, the major premise. After that, it will be determined that this same subject is proven for another thing universally or partially, e.g., proving 'human' for every individual of 'writer' so it is said: 'Every writer is human,' or attaching the knowledge of 'humanity' to some individuals of 'body' so it is said: 'Some bodies are human.' Thus the result of the first premise of qiyas, the minor premise, comes out. Then from combining the minor and major premises, a knowledge is obtained that was not previously had. Indeed, from combining 'Every writer is an animal' and 'Every animal is sentient', it is known that every writer is sentient. Similarly, from 'Every writer is human' and 'Nothing from human is stone', it is known that no individual of writer is stone. Likewise, from 'Some body is human' and 'Every human is animal', it is known that some body is animal. In short, from this clarification and exemplification, it is evident that in this form of qiyas, it is a condition that the minor premise be affirmative and the major premise be universal.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Nu'ut, a commentary on Muslim al-Thubut, focuses on the classification of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by explaining why the text restricts qiyas to only five 'proximate forms' (صور قريبة). He notes that qiyas is of two main types: exceptional (istithna'i) and conjunctive (iqtirani). Conjunctive further divides into categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti). Conditional conjunctive often requires an additional premise to produce a conclusion, so it is not 'proximate'. Even in categorical conjunctive, some forms (like the first figure) produce conclusions that are not immediately obvious to the mind. Therefore, the matn author deliberately omits many forms and focuses only on three forms of categorical conjunctive and two of exceptional — totalling five 'proximate' forms that naturally yield conclusions without external premises. This is a methodological choice to avoid unnecessary lengthening, implicitly critiquing earlier scholars like Ibn Hajib and Ibn al-Humam for delving into all forms including remote ones and conditions of induction. The passage then details the first form, which corresponds to the first figure of Aristotelian logic. In this figure, the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major premises. The condition for a valid conclusion is that the minor must be affirmative and the major universal. This yields four valid moods: (1) affirmative universal minor, affirmative universal major (Barbara); (2) affirmative universal minor, negative universal major (Celarent); (3) affirmative particular minor, affirmative universal major (Darii); (4) affirmative particular minor, negative universal major (Ferio). Examples are given for each, using legal-like terms (animal, human, stone, etc.) to illustrate how a known ruling is extended. In the final summary, the author explains the underlying logic: first identify the middle term's ruling (e.g., every animal is sentient); then establish that the middle term applies to the minor term (e.g., every writer is human); then the major term's ruling transfers to the minor. This is essentially the mechanism of categorical syllogism. The commentator emphasizes the conditions: minor affirmative (because a negative minor would not connect the middle to the minor) and major universal (to ensure the conclusion applies to all or some of the minor). This discussion is foundational for Hanafi reasoning in deriving rulings by qiyas, where the validity of an analogy depends on clear and necessary logical connections.

Key Terms
شكل أول (shakl awwal): The first figure of syllogism where the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major
ضرب (darb): A mood or combination of quantity and quality of premises in a given figure
إنتاج (intaj): The act of deriving a conclusion from premises

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which two conditions must be satisfied for a valid first figure syllogism?
A) Minor must be universal; major must be affirmative.
B) Minor must be affirmative; major must be universal.
C) Minor must be negative; major must be particular.
D) Minor must be particular; major must be negative.

Key Takeaway
The first figure of qiyas requires an affirmative minor and a universal major, yielding four valid moods that produce conclusions necessarily.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Affirmative Minor Premise in the First Figure of Syllogism
Explaining the four types of syllogism and defending the condition of affirmative minor in the first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the minor premise must be affirmative in the first figure syllogism and evaluate the objection from coextensive terms using the Hanafi logical framework.

Core Idea
The first figure syllogism requires an affirmative minor premise for a conclusion that follows directly, without extraneous premises.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, legal analogy (qiyas) is constructed using the first figure of syllogism. This lesson examines a fundamental condition: the minor premise must be affirmative. We will explore the four types of syllogism, the rationale behind the condition, and a critical objection from Imam Ibn al-Humam who argued that coextensive terms allow a negative minor. The commentator refutes this, insisting on the intrinsic validity of the syllogism without external logical principles. This discussion sharpens your understanding of how rational proof (dalil ‘aqli) operates within Hanafi methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of syllogism
Review the four combinations of affirmative/negative and universal/particular premises. The first figure requires a universal major and an affirmative minor for direct validity.
Memorize the four types, focusing on the first figure's conditions.
2
Understand the condition for the first figure
The minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise must be universal. If the minor is negative, the conclusion does not follow by itself; an extra premise is needed.
This is the core rule defended by the commentator.
3
Analyze Ibn al-Humam's objection
He argued that when the two terms of the major premise are coextensive (equal in extension), a negative minor still yields a valid conclusion, as in: 'Nothing from man is a horse; every horse is a neighing animal' → 'Nothing from man is a neighing animal'.
Understand the example and why it seems to work without an affirmative minor.
4
Trace the Hanafi refutation
The commentator argues that this syllogism relies on an extraneous premise: 'negation of one coextensive entails negation of the other'. Without this premise, the conclusion does not follow directly. Hence, the condition of an affirmative minor remains intact.
The key point: validity 'bi dhatihi' (by itself) vs. 'bi ghayrihi' (through another).
5
Apply to legal analogy (qiyas fiqhi)
In Hanafi usul, the logical form controls the validity of analogical reasoning. If the effective cause (illa) is not affirmatively established in the minor premise, the analogy may become invalid unless an external principle is invoked. This shows the practical importance of the condition.
Think of a real fiqh analogy: e.g., wine (asl) is intoxicating (illa), so every intoxicating substance (far') is prohibited.

صغری کے ضمن میں کیا گیا ہے۔
And nothing from the animal (is) a stone, so nothing from the donkey (is) a stone. (3) Third type: composed of a particular affirmative minor premise and a universal affirmative major premise. For example: some animals are human, and every human is rational, so some animals are rational. (4) Fourth type: composed of a particular affirmative minor premise and a universal negative major premise. For example: some animals are lions, and no lion is a neighing animal, so some animals are not neighing. Summary of the author's statement: The summary of the explanation of the first form in the author's words is that first, the affirmative or negative judgment of every individual of something (the middle term) is known (e.g., for every individual of animal, it is known that it is a body). Then that thing (the middle term) is established for the other (the major term) either universally or partially, i.e., for every individual of it or for some individuals (e.g., being animal is established for every individual of man). So the major term is also established affirmatively or negatively for every individual of the minor term or for some individuals, as in the mentioned example 'being a body' is established for every individual of man. For all individuals of a subject, a judgment (affirmative or negative) is known. For example, if the word 'man' is the subject in a proposition, then the judgment of animality is affirmed for all its individuals by saying 'every man is an animal', or the judgment of 'stoneness' is negated from all individuals of 'man' by saying 'nothing from man is a stone'. This is the content of the second premise, the major premise of the syllogism. Then it will be known that this same subject is established for another thing universally or partially, e.g., affirming man for all individuals of 'writer' by saying 'every writer is a man', or attaching the predicate 'man' to some individuals of 'body' by saying 'some bodies are men'. Thus, the content of the first premise, the minor premise, emerges. Then by arranging the minor and major premises, knowledge is obtained that was not previously known. Accordingly, from the arrangement of 'every writer is a man' and 'every man is an animal', it is known that every writer is an animal. Similarly, from 'every writer is a man' and 'nothing from man is a stone', it is known that no individual of a writer is a stone. Likewise, from 'some bodies are men' and 'every man is an animal', it is known that some bodies are animals. In short, from this explanation and illustration, it is clear that in this form of syllogism, the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise universal, otherwise the conclusion will not be correct. His statement: 'And what is in al-Tahrir except in the case of equality of the two terms of the major premise...' In this statement, the author mentions the saying of the author of al-Tahrir, Imam Ibn al-Humam, and refutes it. Because the matn author said that for the correctness of the conclusion in the first form, an affirmative minor is a condition. But contrary to this, Imam Ibn al-Humam said in Tahrir al-Usul that in the case where the two terms of the major premise of the syllogism are equal (coextensive), it is not necessary for the minor premise to be affirmative in the first form. Rather, in the case of equality of the two terms of the major premise, even if the minor premise is negative, a correct conclusion still results, because the negation of one of two coextensive things entails the negation of the other. Thus, the middle term, which is equal to the major term, its negation from the minor term entails the negation of the major term from the minor term. For example, 'nothing from man is a horse' and 'every horse is a neighing animal' – this syllogism is correct, producing the conclusion 'nothing from man is a neighing animal', despite the minor premise not being affirmative, even though the arrangement of the syllogism is given in the first form. Refutation: The author refutes Ibn al-Humam with his statement 'it is nothing' – that his statement is not correct – and he explains the reason for its incorrectness with his statement 'not by itself'. The summary is that the discussion is about a syllogism that entails a third statement essentially and directly. And the syllogism for which Ibn al-Humam claimed correctness in the case of a negative minor premise in the mentioned example does not entail the third statement essentially and directly. Rather, that syllogism yields a correct conclusion through the mediation of an extraneous premise, and it entails the third statement through that extraneous premise. The extraneous premise is: 'The negation of one of two coextensive things entails the negation of the other', or 'The judgment of two coextensive things is one'. Thus, if we disregard the mentioned extraneous premise, this syllogism does not yield a correct conclusion without the condition of an affirmative minor. His statement: 'It was said: A is not B, and everything not B is C' – This statement indicates the objection raised concerning the condition of an affirmative minor in the first form.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the logical structure of syllogism (qiyas) as applied to the first figure (shakl awwal), which is foundational for legal analogy in Hanafi usul. The commentator first describes the four types of syllogism (ضرب) that Aristotle recognized, focusing on the combinations of affirmative/negative and universal/particular premises. The core condition emphasized is that in the first figure, the minor premise (صغری) must be affirmative (موجبة) for a valid conclusion to follow directly. The universal major (کبری کلیہ) is also required. The author then addresses a critical dispute with Imam Ibn al-Humam, who argued that if the two terms of the major premise (subject and predicate) are coextensive (مساوی), then a negative minor premise can still yield a valid conclusion without an affirmative minor. The commentator refutes this by arguing that such a syllogism only works if an extraneous premise (مقدمہ اجنبیہ) is added—specifically, the principle that 'negation of one coextensive entails negation of the other'. Without this external premise, the syllogism is invalid. Thus, the condition of an affirmative minor remains intact, preserving the classical logical rule. This discussion reinforces the importance of proper syllogistic form in legal reasoning (qiyas fiqhi), ensuring that analogical conclusions are sound. The commentator insists on the intrinsic validity of the syllogism without reliance on external logical principles.

Key Terms
شکل اول (shakl awwal): The first figure of syllogism, where the middle term is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise.
صغری (sughra): The minor premise of a syllogism, containing the minor term (asr) and the middle term.
کبری (kubra): The major premise of a syllogism, containing the major term (akbar) and the middle term.
مقدمہ اجنبیہ (muqaddima ajnabiyya): An extraneous premise external to the syllogism that is used to mediate the conclusion when the minor premise is negative.
مساوات (musawat): Coextension or equality between the subject and predicate of the major premise, meaning they have identical extension.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the minor premise must always be universal.
B) Because it requires an extraneous premise that 'negation of one coextensive entails negation of the other'.
C) Because the major premise is not universal.
D) Because the conclusion is false.

Key Takeaway
The first figure syllogism in Hanafi usul requires an affirmative minor premise for intrinsic validity; coextensive major terms do not exempt this condition.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
8 min
Necessity of Affirmative Minor in First-Figure Qiyas: Refuting Ibn al-Humam
Dispute with Ibn al-Humam on condition of affirmative minor in first-figure qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate Ibn al-Humam's argument against the requirement of an affirmative minor in first-figure qiyas and explain the author's refutation using the concepts of a foreign premise and the reclassification of negative minors as affirmative with modified predicates.

Core Idea
A first-figure qiyas without an affirmative minor is not directly productive; it requires a foreign premise or a reinterpretation of the minor as affirmative.

In this passage, the commentator of Ashrun Nu'ut defends the matn's position that an affirmative minor premise is essential for a directly productive first-figure syllogism. Ibn al-Humam, a renowned Hanafi usuli, challenged this by proposing cases where equality of the major's terms allows a negative minor to suffice. The author systematically refutes this, exposing hidden assumptions and clarifying the logical structure of qiyas. This lesson dissects the dispute and sharpens your ability to analyze syllogistic reasoning in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn's claim and Ibn al-Humam's objection
The matn states that a valid first-figure qiyas requires an affirmative minor premise. Ibn al-Humam objects, arguing that when the major premise has equal subject and predicate (mutasawiyan), a negative minor suffices.
The equality condition refers to terms that are coextensive (e.g., 'horse' and 'whinnying' are not equal; 'not-B' and 'C' in some contexts may be equal).
2
Analyze Ibn al-Humam's counterexample
Ibn al-Humam gives: 'Nothing of man is a horse, every horse is whinnying' → 'Nothing of man is whinnying'. He claims this works despite a negative minor. Notice that the major's terms 'horse' and 'whinnying' are not equal in extension, so the example does not truly satisfy the equality condition.
The commentator points out the false equality, undermining the example's relevance.
3
Evaluate the author's refutation using the foreign premise
The author argues that even if the major had equal terms, the conclusion would only follow if we implicitly assume the foreign premise 'negation of one equal necessitates negation of the other'. Without it, the syllogism is not directly productive (muntij bi al-dhat).
A foreign premise (muqaddima ajnabiyya) is an additional proposition outside the original syllogism needed to make the entailment valid.
4
Examine the objection 'A is not B, everything not B is C'
An objector presents: 'A is not B, everything not B is C' → 'A is C'. This appears to validate Ibn al-Humam's claim without any foreign premise. The author responds by analyzing the nature of the minor premise.
The major 'everything not B is C' treats 'not B' as a subject of which 'C' is affirmed.
5
Understand the reclassification of the minor as affirmative with modified predicate
The author explains that the minor 'A is not B' is not a simple negative; rather, because the major affirms 'not B' of subjects, the minor actually affirms the attribute 'not B' of A. Thus, it is an affirmative proposition with a modified predicate (mujiba ma'dulat al-hamul). This preserves the condition of an affirmative minor.
Otherwise, the minor term A would not be included under the middle term 'not B', and the syllogism would fail.

ہو جائے گالہذا ماننا پڑے گا کہ مثال مذکور میں صغری سالبہ نہیں ہے بلکہ موجہ معدولۃ المعد ول ہے۔ متن المسلم : - "الثانيه ان يعلم حكم لكل افراد شنى و مقابله للآخر كله او بعضه فيعلم سلب ذالك الشئى عن الآخر كذالك بتأمل ومافى المختصر ان لا انتاج الا بالاول فادعاء لان اللزوم لالمقدمة اجنبية يجوز أن يكون مع متعدد والد
Then the result will not be correct. His saying: 'And what is in al-Tahrir except in equality of the two sides of the major, etc.' In this expression, the author has mentioned the statement of the author of al-Tahrir, Imam Ibn al-Humam, and then refuted it. Because the matn said that for the first figure to produce a correct result, an affirmative minor premise is a condition. Contrary to that, Imam Ibn al-Humam said in Tahrir al-Usul that when the two sides (subject and predicate) of the major premise of the qiyas are equal, it is not necessary for the minor premise to be affirmative in the first figure; rather, if the minor is negative, a sound result still emerges because the negation of one of two equal terms necessitates the negation of the other. So the middle term, which is equal to the major's subject, its negation from the minor necessitates the negation of the major from the minor. For example: 'Nothing of man is a horse, and every horse is whinnying' — this qiyas is sound, producing the result 'Nothing of man is whinnying,' even though the minor is not affirmative, while the qiyas is arranged in the first figure. Refutation: The author refuted Ibn al-Humam with his saying 'it is not anything' to indicate that his statement is incorrect, and with his saying 'not by itself' to explain the reason for its incorrectness. The gist is that the discussion pertains to a qiyas that necessarily entails the third proposition by itself and without mediation. And the qiyas for which Ibn al-Humam claimed correctness and productivity in the mentioned example when the minor is negative does not necessarily entail the third proposition by itself; rather, that qiyas gives a correct result through the mediation of a foreign premise and necessitates the third proposition through the foreign premise, which is 'the negation of one of two equal terms necessitates the negation of the other, or the ruling for equal terms is one.' So when we disregard that foreign premise, this same qiyas does not yield a correct result without the condition of an affirmative minor. His saying: 'He presented: A is not B, and everything not B is C' — With this expression, the matn referred to the objection that has been raised regarding the condition of an affirmative minor in the first figure. The explanation of the objection is: Suppose a qiyas whose minor is negative and whose major and its two sides (subject and predicate) are equal; then such a qiyas yields a correct result even though the affirmative minor is missing. For example: 'A is not B, and everything not B is C', so the result 'A is C' is a correct qiyas even though its minor is negative. A clear example: 'Three is not even, and everything not even is odd — therefore three is odd.' This shows that an affirmative minor is not a condition in the first figure; otherwise, when it is negative the result would never emerge. His saying: 'And the answer is that negation insofar as it is negation, etc.' — With this expression, he intends to answer the mentioned objection. The gist of the answer: The minor that appears outwardly negative in your presented qiyas is in reality not negative; rather, that minor is affirmative with a negated predicate, in other words affirmative with a modified predicate. Because negation as such is pure removal, and the major in the mentioned qiyas, i.e., 'everything not B is C', does not involve a pure removal contract such that the minor could be called negative; rather, it involves the affirmation of something about something. Since 'everything not B' means every thing for which 'not B' is affirmed, i.e., that 'not B' is established for it. So when we consider that affirmation in the minor, the minor does not remain negative; rather, it becomes an affirmative with a modified predicate. Otherwise the qiyas would not be productive, because on that assumption the minor term would not be included under the middle term. Since in the qiyas the minor term is 'A' and the middle term is 'not B', and the individuals and instances of 'not B' are those for which 'not B' is established. On the assumption of considering the minor as negative, it is not known that 'not B' is established for the minor term, because in the minor there is no prior affirmation. So when 'not B' is not established for the minor, how can the minor be included under the middle term? But when one takes into consideration the affirmation of 'not B' in the minor, then the minor will be included under the middle term. Therefore, it must be acknowledged that in the mentioned example the minor is not negative but rather affirmative with a modified predicate. The Matn of Muslim: "The second: that the ruling is known for all individuals of one thing and its opposite for the other entirely or partially, so the negation of that thing from the other is known likewise through reflection. And what is in al-Mukhtasar is that there is no productiveness except through the first, so the claim that the entailment is through a foreign premise is allowed to be with multiple and the..."
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) within a Sharh on Muslim al-Thubut, where the author challenges a view of Imam Ibn al-Humam (author of al-Tahrir) regarding the necessity of an affirmative minor premise in the first figure of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in Hanafi usul. The matn (base text) had stated that an affirmative minor (ijab sughra) is a condition for a valid first-figure syllogism. Ibn al-Humam argued that when the two sides (subject and predicate) of the major premise are equal (musawat tarafay al-kubra), an affirmative minor is not necessary; even a negative minor yields a valid result because negation of one equal necessitates negation of the other, citing the example: 'Nothing of man is a horse, and every horse is whinnying' produces 'Nothing of man is whinnying'. The author refutes this, stating that such a syllogism does not produce the result directly (bi dhatihi) but only through a foreign premise (muqaddima ajnabiyya), namely the rule that 'negation of one of two equals necessitates negation of the other'. Without that implicit premise, the syllogism fails. Then an objection is raised: what about a syllogism like 'A is not B, and everything not B is C'? This yields 'A is C' despite a negative minor. The author answers by analyzing the nature of negation in the major premise: 'everything not B is C' actually affirms the predicate 'not B' of subjects, so the minor 'A is not B' is not a simple negative but an affirmative with a modified predicate (mujiba ma'dulat al-hamul), because it affirms the negation for A. Thus the minor is effectively affirmative when the major is considered properly. The passage concludes with a portion of the matn from Muslim al-Thubut stating the second condition for qiyas (knowledge of ruling for all individuals of a thing and its opposite), noting that according to al-Mukhtasar, productiveness only occurs in the first figure, and that claims of entailment through a foreign premise may be valid with multiple premises.

Key Terms
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning or syllogism in usul al-fiqh; a compound argument composed of two premises (minor and major) that entails a conclusion.
صغری (sughra): The minor premise in a syllogism; the proposition that contains the minor term and the middle term.
کبری (kubra): The major premise in a syllogism; the proposition that contains the middle term and the major term.
ایجاب صغری (ijab sughra): An affirmative (positive) minor premise as opposed to a negative (saliba) one; essential for a directly productive first-figure syllogism in Hanafi usul.
منتج (muntij): Productive; a syllogism that correctly entails its conclusion by itself (bi al-dhat).
متساوین (mutasawiyan): Two terms that are equal in extension (coextensive); the negation of one necessitates the negation of the other.
مقدمہ اجنبیہ (muqaddima ajnabiyya): A foreign premise; an additional proposition outside the original syllogism that is needed to make the entailment valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author of this commentary, why is the syllogism 'A is not B, everything not B is C' considered to have an affirmative minor rather than a negative one?
A) Because the major premise 'everything not B is C' makes 'not B' a predicate that is affirmed of A, so 'A is not B' affirms the attribute 'not B' for A, making it an affirmative with a modified predicate.
B) Because the negation in the minor is a simple negation, and the major has an affirmative form, so the minor remains negative but still productive.
C) Because the Hanafi school does not distinguish between affirmative and negative minors in the first figure.
D) Because Ibn al-Humam proved that an affirmative minor is not necessary.

Key Takeaway
A first-figure qiyas requires an affirmative minor for direct productiveness; apparent counterexamples either involve a foreign premise or a hidden affirmative form.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
10 min
First Figure Minor Premise: Affirmative or Negative?
Objection and response regarding necessity of affirmative minor premise in first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the necessity of an affirmative minor premise in the first figure of syllogism, explain the logical distinction between a true negative and an affirmative with a negated predicate, and apply this to evaluate analogical arguments (qiyas) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
The first figure requires an affirmative minor; apparent negatives are affirmatives with negated predicates.

This passage addresses a critical objection to the standard rule that the first figure of syllogism requires an affirmative minor premise. The objector presents an example that seems to produce a valid conclusion from a negative minor. The Hanafi response, grounded in logical precision, reinterprets the example to maintain consistency. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying qiyas in deriving legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objector's claim: a first-figure syllogism with a negative minor premise can still yield a valid conclusion. Note the example given: 'الثلثة ليس بزوج وكل ماليس بزوج فرد - الثلثة فرد'.
2
Analyze the example
Examine the minor premise 'الثلثة ليس بزوج' (the three is not an even number). Is it truly a negative proposition? Consider the logical form.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
The commentator argues that the minor premise is not negative but affirmative with a negated predicate (موجبة معدولة المحمول). The major premise 'كل ماليس بزوج فرد' affirms oddness to everything that has the attribute 'not even'. Thus, the minor affirms that the three has that attribute.
The key is that 'ليس ب' in the major premise is not mere negation but an affirmative attribution.
4
Understand the logical distinction
Distinguish between a true negative (salb) that denies any predicate, and an affirmative with a negated predicate (mujaba ma'dulat al-mahmul) that affirms a negative attribute. In the example, the minor is of the latter type.
This distinction preserves the rule that the minor must be affirmative.
5
Apply to qiyas
In legal analogy (qiyas), the first figure is used to derive rulings. Ensuring the minor premise is affirmative (even if via a negated predicate) guarantees that the minor term (the new case) falls under the middle term (the effective cause). This maintains the validity of the analogy.
Without this analysis, a student might incorrectly accept invalid analogies.

اعتراض کی تقریر یہ ہے کہ کوئی قیاس ایسا ہو جس کا صفری سالبہ ہو اور کبری اور اس کا دونوں طرف ( موضوع محمول ) برابر ہوں تو ایسے قیاس کا نتیجہ صحیح ہوتا ہے حالانکہ ایجاب صغری منمی ہے۔ جیسے ليس ب، وكل مالیس ب ج فالنتیجہ آج صحیح قیاس ہے جب کہ اس کا صغری سالیہ ہے تو ضیح مثال کے لئے یہ کہہ سکتے ہیں ۔ الثلثة ليس بزوج وكل ماليس بزوج فرد - الثلثة فرد معلوم ہوا کہ شکل اول میں ایجاب صغری شرط نہیں ہے ورنہ سالیہ ہوتے ہوئے نتیجہ ہر گز برآمد نہ ہوتا۔ قوله - والجواب أن السلب من حيث هوهو الخ اس عبارت سے اعتراض مذکور کا جواب دینا چاہتے ہیں جواب : کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ تمہارے پیش کردہ قیاس میں جو صغری بظا ہر سالبہ نظر آرہا ہے درحقیقت وہ سالبہ نہیں ہے بلکہ وہ صغری موجب سالبتہ الحمول بلفظ دیگر موجبہ معدولہ الحمول ہے کیونکہ سلب بحیثیت ساب رفع محض کا نام ہے اور قیاس مذکور کا جو کبری یعنی کل مالیس ب ج ہے اس میں عقد وضع رفع محض نہیں کہ صغریٰ کو سالیہ کہا جائے بلکہ اس میں ثبوت شئی لشنی ملحوظ ہے، چونکہ کل مالیس ب سے مراد ہر وہ شئی ہے جس پر لیس ب یا یعنی اس کے لئے لیس ب ثابت ہو پس ہم جب اسی ثبوت کو صغری میں اعتبار کر لیتے ہیں تو صغری سالبہ نہیں رہ جاتا بلکہ صغری موجبہ معدولتہ الحمول ہو جاتا ہے وگر نہ قیاس منتج ہی نہ ہوگا اس لئے کہ اس تقدیر پر اصغر اوسط کے تحت مندرج نہ ہوگا چونکہ قیاس میں اصغر " " ہے اور اوسط "مالیس ب" ہے اور لیس سب کے افراد و مصداق وہ ہیں جس کے لئے لیس ب ثابت ہے اور صغریٰ کو سالبہ ماننے کی تقدیر پر سے یہ معلوم نہیں ہوتا ہے کہ اصغر کے لئے لیس ب ثابت ہے کیونکہ صغری میں پہلے سے ثبوت محفوظ ہی نہیں پس جب اصغر میں لیس ب ثابت نہیں تو اصغر اوسط کے تحت کیونکر مندرج ہو گا مگر جب لیس بے کے ثبوت کا لحاظ صغری میں کر لیا جائے گا تو اصغر اوسط کے تحت مندرج ہو جائے گالہذا ماننا پڑے گا کہ مثال مذکور میں صغری سالبہ نہیں ہے بلکہ موجہ معدولۃ المعد ول ہے۔
The objection is presented as follows: There is a syllogism whose minor premise is negative and the major and its both terms (subject and predicate) are equal, then the conclusion of such a syllogism is correct, even though the minor premise is negative. For example: 'ليس ب' (not B), and 'كل ماليس ب ج' (everything that is not B is C), then the conclusion is a valid syllogism, while its minor premise is negative. As an example, we can say: 'The three is not an even number, and everything that is not an even number is an odd number - the three is an odd number.' It became clear that in the first figure, an affirmative minor premise is not a condition; otherwise, if it were negative, the conclusion would never be produced. His statement: 'And the answer is that negation in itself...' etc. Through this phrase, he intends to answer the mentioned objection. The answer: The summary is that in the syllogism you presented, the minor premise that appears to be negative is in reality not negative, but rather it is an affirmative with a negated predicate, in other words an affirmative with a negated copula. Because negation as such is mere removal, and in the major premise of the mentioned syllogism, i.e., 'كل ماليس ب ج', the attribution is not mere removal such that the minor could be called negative, but rather the affirmation of a thing for a thing is considered. Since 'كل ماليس ب' means every thing for which 'ليس ب' is affirmed, i.e., for which 'ليس ب' is established, then when we take this affirmation into account in the minor premise, the minor premise no longer remains negative, but becomes affirmative with a negated predicate. Otherwise, the syllogism would not produce a conclusion at all, because on that estimation, the minor term would not be subsumed under the middle term. Since in the syllogism the minor term is '...' and the middle term is 'ماليس ب', and the individuals and referents of 'ليس ب' are those for which 'ليس ب' is established. And on the assumption that the minor premise is negative, it is not known that 'ليس ب' is established for the minor term, because in the minor premise affirmation is not preserved. So when 'ليس ب' is not established in the minor, how can the minor be subsumed under the middle? But when the affirmation of 'ليس ب' is taken into account in the minor, then the minor becomes subsumed under the middle. Therefore, it must be accepted that in the mentioned example, the minor premise is not negative, but affirmative with a negated predicate.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents a logical objection and its resolution in the context of Aristotelian syllogisms used in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The objector argues that a first-figure syllogism can produce a valid conclusion even when the minor premise is negative, using the example: 'The three is not an even number; everything that is not an even number is an odd number; therefore, the three is an odd number.' This seems to contradict the standard rule that the first figure requires an affirmative minor premise. The commentator, Al-Qari (assuming this is from Ashraf al-Nu'ut), responds by analyzing the logical form. He argues that the minor premise 'The three is not an even number' is not truly a negative proposition (salb). Instead, it is an affirmative proposition with a negated predicate (mujaba ma'dulat al-mahmul). The major premise 'Everything that is not an even number is an odd number' does not express mere negation; it affirms a property (oddness) to everything that has the property of 'not being an even number'. Thus, the middle term is actually 'ماليس ب' which refers to everything that has the attribute of 'not being even'. When we consider the minor term 'three', it falls under this middle term because it possesses that attribute. Therefore, the minor premise is effectively affirmative (affirming that the three has the attribute 'not even'), and the syllogism conforms to the first-figure rule. The commentator emphasizes that if the minor premise were truly negative, the minor term would not be subsumed under the middle term, and no conclusion could follow.

Key Terms
سلب (salb): A negative proposition that denies something of something else.
موجبة معدولة المحمول (mujaba ma'dulat al-mahmul): An affirmative proposition with a negated predicate, e.g., 'A is not-B'.
شكل اول (shakl awwal): The first figure of syllogism where the middle is subject in the major and predicate in the minor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the objection, the syllogism 'الثلثة ليس بزوج وكل ماليس بزوج فرد' produces which conclusion?
A) الثلثة زوج
B) الثلثة فرد
C) الثلثة ليس بفرد
D) الثلثة ليس بزوج

Key Takeaway
The first figure of syllogism requires an affirmative minor premise; apparent negatives like 'A is not B' are actually affirmatives with negated predicates, preserving logical consistency in qiyas.

The Second Figure of Syllogism and Its Four Valid Moods
Definition and four valid moods of the second figure of syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the second figure of syllogism, state its four valid moods, and evaluate why it is considered productive in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
The second figure yields a valid conclusion when the major premise is universal and the premises differ in quality.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, logical syllogism (qiyas) is used to derive rulings. The second figure is one of the four figures, particularly useful for establishing negative conclusions. This passage defines it, outlines its conditions, lists its four valid moods, and refutes the claim that only the first figure yields conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure
The second figure is defined by the middle term being the predicate in both premises. Identify the middle term's position in the examples given.
This distinguishes it from the first figure where the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major.
2
Identify the key condition
The premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. Trace this condition in the four moods listed.
Violating this condition invalidates the syllogism.
3
Memorize the four valid moods
List the four moods: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major; (2) universal negative minor + universal affirmative major; (3) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major; (4) particular negative minor + universal affirmative major. Note the conclusion type for each.
All conclusions are negative; the minor can be particular but the major must be universal.
4
Analyze the refutation
The passage rejects Ibn Hajib's claim that only the first figure is productive. Understand the Hanafi argument that necessary consequence is possible in multiple figures without an extraneous premise.
This supports the use of the second figure in fiqh reasoning.

Source Text
