Critique of the Mukhtasar's Proof and Superiority of the Author's Alternative
Critical analysis of a rational proof regarding human action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to ANALYSE a rational proof (dalil aqli) in Hanafi usul by identifying its missing category and EVALUATE a refined alternative that avoids that flaw.

Core Idea
A proof that ignores preferred action (awlawi) cannot prove compulsion; the refined proof captures all categories.

In usul al-fiqh, rational proofs (adilla aqliyya) are used to establish theological foundations for legal theory. This passage examines a proof from al-Mukhtasar which argues that human actions are involuntary, thereby negating inherent moral value. The commentator then presents a superior proof from the author of Musallam al-Thubut. We will follow the critique that reveals a missing category—preferred action (awlawi)—and see how the alternative avoids this flaw while being more concise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its target
What is the proposition being proven? The claim: 'The action of the slave is not voluntary, so it cannot be good or evil by itself.' Determine the context: this supports the Ash'ari/Hanafi position that moral value is divinely ascribed, not intrinsic.
2
Reconstruct the Mukhtasar's proof
Break down the proof: Actions are either necessary (lazim) or permissible (ja'iz). If necessary, the agent is compelled. If permissible, either it requires a preponderant (leading to infinite regress) or it does not (accidental). In both sub-branches the action is not freely chosen.
The proof attempts to exhaust all possibilities.
3
Locate the critique: the missing category
The commentator (Mirza Jan) objects that the proof is 'based on negating preference (awlawiyya).' Identify what is omitted: the category of preferred action (awlawi), which is above permissible but below obligatory. Such an action occurs due to a preponderant without being necessary or accidental.
Why is this a problem? Without negating awlawi, the proof does not show that all possible actions are compelled.
4
Understand the author's refinement
Examine the author's proof: When a preponderant exists, if the action is preferred (awlawi), its occurrence is possible, but the non-occurrence is impossible (preferring the preponderated is impossible). Thus the action is compelled without needing to negate awlawi. This avoids the regress problem.
The key move: impossibility of preferring the preponderated (tarjih al-marjuh).
5
Compare the two proofs
Assess why the author's proof is considered 'better and more concise.' It does not rely on negating preference, covers all cases (including awlawi), and avoids the unnecessary mention of 'accidental' which is problematic because possibility requires a cause.
Conclude: the refined proof is logically sounder and more elegant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وهذا احسن واخصر ممافى المختصر
اس عبارت سے ماتن نے دلیل مذکور کی عمدگی اور جامعیت و اختصار کو بیان کیا ہے کہتے ہیں کہ یہ دلیل ثالث جو شرح مختصر میں مرزا جان کے حاشیہ سے ماخوذ ہے مختصر ابن حاجب میں ذکر کردہ دلیل کے بہ نسبت بڑی عمدہ اور نہایت ہی مختصر دلیل ہے ، چنانچہ ماتن نے اس دلیل کی عمدگی اور اس کے کمال اختصار پر گفتگو کرتے ہوئے اور مختصر ابن صاحب کی دلیل پر نقص و طوالت کا ذکر کرتے ہوئے حاشیہ میں کہا ہے ۔
قال في المختصر واستدل فعل العبد غير مختار فلايكون حسنا ولا قبيحاً لذاته اجماعاً لانه اذا كان واجباً فى نسخة (لازما) فواضح وان كان جائزاً فان افتقر الى مرجح عاد التقسيم والا فهو اتفاقى وتقرير ذالک فی شرح العضدی ولا يخفى عليك انه يرد عليه انه مبنى على نفى الاولوية وربما يمنع كونه اتفاقياً لوجود السبب وفيه مافيه انتهت
مختصر میں ذکر کردہ دلیل کی توضیح و تقریر شرح عضدی میں مندرجہ ذیل الفاظ کے ساتھ مذکور ہے جن کو فاضل خیر آبادی وغیرہ شراح مسلم نے ذکر کیا ہے۔
وهو أن الفعل ان كان لازم الصدور عن العبد بحيث لا يمكنه الترك فظاهر انه غير مختار بل فعله اضطرارى وان كان جائزا وجوده وعدمه فان افتقرالی مرجح فمع المرجع يعود التقسيم فيه بان يقال ان كان لازماً فاضطراري والا احتاج الى مرجح آخر ولزم التسلسل وان لم يفتقر الى مرجح بل يصدر عنه تارة ولا يصدر أخرى مع تساوى الحالتين من غير تجدد امر من الفاعل فهو اتفاقي انتهى. یعنی بندہ کا فعل غیر مختار ہے لہذا وہ لذاتہ حسن و قبیح نہیں ہوسکتا کیونکہ فعل عبد اس تقدیر پر یا تو واجب ہوگا کہ بندہ سے لازماً اسکا صدور ہو گا اس حیثیت سے کہ بندہ کو اس کا ترک ممکن نہیں تب تو ظاہر ہے کہ بندہ غیر مختار ہے اور اس کا فعل اضطراری ہے یا یہ ہے کہ فعل عبد کا وجود عدم دونوں جائز ہوگا تو وہ کسی مرج[ح؟] کا محتاج ہے یا نہیں اگر مرج[ح؟] کا محتاج ہے تو تقسیم مرجح کے ساتھ پلٹے گی بایں طور کہ یہ فعل مع المريح لازم و واجب ہے یا جائز ، بر تقدیر اول اضطراری ہوگا اور بر تقدیر ثانی مریج آخر کا محتاج ہوگا ، پھر اس مرجع آخر پر کلام جاری ہوگا کہ واجب ہے یا جائز ، اگر واجب ہے تو اضطراری اور جائز ہے تو مرجح ثالث کا محتاج ہوگا ، اس طرح تسلسل لازم آئے گا اور اگر مرج[ح؟] کا محتاج نہیں ہے کہ فعل عبد کا صدور کبھی ہوتا ہے اور کبھی نہیں ہوتا ہے اس کی دونوں حالت، فاعل کی طرف سے کسی تجدد کے بغیر، برابر ہے تو وہ اتفاقی ہے۔ قوله في الحاشيه ولا يخفى عليك انه يردانه مبنى على نفى الاولوية مختصر کی دلیل پر اعتراض کرتے ہوئے فاضل مرزا جان نے شرح مختصر کے حاشیہ میں کہا ہے " انه مبنی على نفى الاولوية" اعتراض : کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ مختصر میں ذکر کردہ دلیل فعل عبد کے اضطراری ہونے پر کافی نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ دلیل نفی اولیت پر موقوف ہے یعنی اس دلیل میں صرف بندہ کے فعل وجوبی اور اتفاقی کو بیان کیا گیا ہے اور فعل اولیٰ جو جواز سے بالا اور وجوب سے کمتر ہوتا ہے کونہیں بیان کیا ہے اس لئے ایسا ممکن ہے کہ بندہ کا فعل مرجح کے ساتھ اسکا صدور اولی ہو اور عدم صدور جائز ہو تو اس صورت میں بندہ سے فعل کا صدور بغیر وجوب واتفاق کے ہوگا اور بندہ مضطر بھی نہ ہوالہذ ادلیل مختصر مذکور کا اضطرار پر تمامیت کے لئے ضروری ہے کہ اولیت کی بھی نفی کی جائے جبکہ دلیل مختصر سے اس کی نفی نہیں ہورہی ہے۔ نیز فعل ممکن کو اتفاقی کہنا منع ہے اس لئے کہ امکان علت مرفقہ کا محتاج ہوتا ہے اور جب علت مرجمہ کے سبب اس فعل کا وجود ہوا تو اتفاقی نہیں ہوسکتا یا جو علت مرجمہ کا محتاج ہو وہ اتفاقی نہیں ہوتا۔
اس کے برخلاف مصنف نے جو طرز استدلال اختیار کیا ہے وہ استدلال نفی اولیت پر موقوف نہیں ہے کیونکہ راج جبکہ اولی غیر واجب ہو تو اولی کا وقوع جائز ہے اور اولی کے ہوتے ہوئے غیر اولی کا وقوع محال ہے کہ وہ مرجوح کی ترجیح ہے اور مرجوح کی ترجیح محال۔ پس اس اعتبار سے مصنف کی دلیل مختصر کی دلیل سے احسن ہے نیز الفاظ وعبارات کے اعتبار سے مختصر کی دلیل طویل ہے جیسا کہ ظاہر ہے علاوہ ازیں اتفاقی کا قول زائد غیر مناسب ہے اس لحاظ سے مسلم میں ذکر کردہ دلیل مختصر سے بھی مختصر ہے۔ قوله في الحاشيه فيه مافيه مختصر کی دلیل کے ایک جز واتفاقی ہونے پر اعتراض
His saying: 'And this is better and more concise than what is in al-Mukhtasar.' Through this expression, the author (al-Matin) has explained the excellence, comprehensiveness, and brevity of the mentioned proof. He says that this third proof, taken from the commentary of Mirza Jan's gloss on Sharh al-Mukhtasar, is much better and extremely concise compared to the proof mentioned in Mukhtasar Ibn al-Hajib. Thus, the author, while discussing the excellence and extreme brevity of this proof and mentioning the deficiency and length of the proof of the author of al-Mukhtasar, said in the gloss: 'He said in al-Mukhtasar: And he argued: The action of the slave is not voluntary, so it cannot be good or evil by itself, by consensus, because if it is wajib (in one version 'necessary'), then it is obvious [that he is compelled], and if it is permissible, then if it needs a preponderant (murajjih), the division returns, otherwise it is accidental (ittifaqi). And its explanation is in Sharh al-'Adudi. And it is not hidden from you that it is objected that it is based on the negation of preference (awlawiyya), and perhaps its being accidental is prevented due to the existence of a cause, and therein lies what lies therein, end.' The explanation and exposition of the proof mentioned in al-Mukhtasar, as given in Sharh al-'Adudi, has been mentioned by Fadil Khayrabadi and other commentators of Musallam in the following words: 'That is, if the action is necessarily proceeding from the slave such that he cannot refrain, then it is clear that he is not voluntary but rather his action is compelled (idhtirari). And if its existence and non-existence are both possible, then if it needs a preponderant, with the preponderant the division returns: it is said if it is necessary then it is compelled, otherwise it needs another preponderant leading to infinite regress. And if it does not need a preponderant, but sometimes proceeds from him and sometimes does not, with the two states being equal without any new act from the agent, then it is accidental.' That is, the slave's action is not voluntary; therefore it cannot be good or evil by itself. Because the slave's action is either such that it necessarily proceeds from the slave, in which case the slave cannot refrain, then it is clear that the slave is not voluntary and his action is compelled. Or both existence and non-existence of the slave's action are possible. Then it either needs a preponderant or not. If it needs a preponderant, then the division returns with the preponderant: that this action with the preponderant is necessary and wajib, or permissible. In the first case it is compelled, in the second it needs another preponderant, and so on infinitely. And if it does not need a preponderant, such that the slave's action sometimes occurs and sometimes does not, with both states equal without any new act from the agent, then it is accidental. His saying in the gloss: 'And it is not hidden from you that it is objected that it is based on the negation of preference.' Objecting to the proof of al-Mukhtasar, Fadil Mirza Jan said in his gloss on Sharh al-Mukhtasar: 'It is based on the negation of preference.' The summary of the objection: The proof mentioned in al-Mukhtasar is not sufficient to prove that the slave's action is compelled, because this proof is dependent on negating preference. That is, in this proof only the obligatory and accidental actions of the slave are mentioned, but the preferred action (al-fi'l al-awlawi), which is above permissible and below obligatory, is not mentioned. Therefore, it is possible that the slave's action occurs as preferred with a preponderant, and its non-occurrence is permissible. In that case, the slave's action occurs without obligation or accident, and the slave is not compelled. So for the completeness of the proof of compulsion, it is necessary to negate preference as well, but the proof from al-Mukhtasar does not negate it. Also, calling a possible action accidental is prevented, because possibility needs a concomitant cause, and when the action exists due to that cause, it cannot be accidental; whatever needs a cause cannot be accidental. In contrast, the method of deduction adopted by the author (al-Matin) is not dependent on negating preference, because when the preponderant, while being preferable but not obligatory, its occurrence is permissible, and with the presence of the preferable, the occurrence of the non-preferable is impossible, because that would be preferring the preponderated, which is impossible. Thus, from this perspective, the author's proof is better than the proof of al-Mukhtasar. Also, in terms of wording and phrasing, the proof of al-Mukhtasar is lengthy, as is apparent. Moreover, the statement about 'accidental' is an unnecessary addition. In this respect, the proof mentioned in Musallam is even more concise than al-Mukhtasar. His saying in the gloss: 'Therein lies what lies therein' is an objection to one part of the proof of al-Mukhtasar, namely its being accidental.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of a proof presented in al-Mukhtasar regarding the involuntary nature of human actions, and a demonstration of the superiority of the author's own proof. The base text of Musallam al-Thubut had stated that the author's proof is better and more concise. The commentary explains that the proof in question is derived from Mirza Jan's gloss on Sharh al-Mukhtasar. It then reproduces the proof from al-Mukhtasar, which argues that a human action must be either necessary (lazim) or permissible (ja'iz). If necessary, the agent is compelled. If permissible, either it requires a preponderant (murajjih) leading to infinite regress, or it does not and is therefore accidental; in either case, the agent is not truly voluntary. The commentary points out a key objection: this proof is based on negating preference (awlawiyya), meaning it only considers two categories (necessary and permissible/accidental) but overlooks the category of preferred actions, which are above permissible but below obligatory. If an action occurs as preferred (due to a preponderant), it is neither obligatory nor accidental, and the agent remains free. Thus the Mukhtasar proof is insufficient. The author's proof, on the other hand, does not rely on negating preference. It holds that when a preponderant is present, if the preferred (awlawi) is not obligatory, its occurrence is permissible; but the occurrence of the non-preferred (mawjuh) is impossible because that would be preferring the preponderated. Hence the author's proof is both more comprehensive and more concise. This discussion is a good example of how Hanafi usul scholars critically evaluate and refine rational proofs, particularly in theological epistemology (the nature of human action).

Key Terms
اولوية (awlawiyya): The quality of being preferred (awlawi); a category of action that is above permissible (ja'iz) and below obligatory (wajib).
مرجح (murajjih): A preponderant factor that tips the balance for the occurrence of a possible action.
اتفاقى (ittifaqi): Accidental; an action that occurs without a preponderant, happening sometimes and not others without any new cause.
اضطرارى (idhtirari): Compelled; an action that necessarily proceeds from the agent such that he cannot refrain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the proof from al-Mukhtasar to be insufficient?
A) Because it relies on a hadith that is weak in transmission.
B) Because it fails to address the category of 'preferred' (awlawi) actions.
C) Because it uses the concept of 'accidental' (ittifaqi) which is not recognized in Hanafi fiqh.
D) Because it contradicts the consensus of the Companions.

Key Takeaway
For a rational proof to be sound, it must cover all categories; neglecting the preferred (awlawi) leaves a gap that undermines the conclusion of compulsion.

The Hanafi Proof: Actions Become Compelled When All Prerequisites Are Present
This passage refutes the Ash'ari doctrine of compulsion in human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rational argument that if all prerequisites for an action are present, the action is compelled (iḍṭirārī), and distinguish between ikhtiyārī and iḍṭirārī actions according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
If all conditions for an action exist, its non-existence is impossible — the action is compelled, and compulsion negates rational good and evil.

This passage presents a rational proof that human actions, when all prerequisites are present, are not voluntary but compelled. The concept of 'obligation by choice' (wujūb bil-ikhtiyār) is introduced to show that even intention becomes necessary. This challenges the Ash'ari notion of conventional intention and sets the stage for the debate on free will and moral responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Follow the first argument
Identify the logical chain: when the matter that triggers intention is realized, intention becomes obligatory (wājib), and then the action becomes necessary. This necessity is called compulsion (iḍṭirār).
Note the key distinction: the action is attributed to the servant but the obligation to act stems from a cause beyond his control.
2
Define ikhtiyārī and iḍṭirārī
From the text, extract the definition: ikhtiyārī is an action whose performance and abandonment are both permissible; iḍṭirārī is an action whose abandonment is impossible. Apply these definitions to the argument.
The text explicitly states: 'اختیاری وہ ہے جس کا فعل و ترک دونوں جائز ہو'.
3
Examine the second exposition
Analyze the rational dichotomy: either all prerequisites exist or they don't. If they exist, the action must exist, otherwise preponderance without a preponderator (tarjīḥ bilā murjīḥ) occurs, which is impossible. Hence the action is compelled.
This is a qiyas argument: the impossibility of the opposite proves the necessity.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: if actions are compelled, there is no rational good or evil (ḥusn wa quḥ 'aqlī) in them, which affects the basis for reward and punishment.
The text says: 'اور اضطراری میں حسن و فتح نہیں ہوتا' (and in compulsion there is no good or evil).
5
Recognize the three objections
The author acknowledges three objections that could be raised: (1) loss of rational good/evil in all actions, (2) God's freedom compromised, (3) difficulty of reward/punishment. These objections set up the Ash'ari defense.
These objections are not refuted in this part; they are acknowledged and will be addressed later.

(۱) افعال عباد اور افعال باری تعالٰی میں حسن و فتح عقلی نہیں رہ جائے گا۔

(۲) باری تعالیٰ اپنے افعال میں مختار مطلق نہیں رہ جائے گا بلکہ وہ ایسا مختار ہوگا جو شائبہ
ایجاب سے متصف ہوگا جیسا کہ بعض معتزلہ اور فلاسفہ کا مختار ہے کہ باری تعالیٰ فاعل موجب ہے اور
جمہور متکلمین اس کے خلاف گئے ہیں۔

(۳) لازم آئے گا بندہ کا اپنے فعل میں مضطر مجبور ہونا اسطرح آخرت ومعاد میں ثواب
و عقاب کے مجازات کا حکم دشوار ہو جائے گا۔

قوله فى الحاشية اتمامه على الاشاعره ممنوع

مصنف کا جواب فاضل مرزا جان نے اشاعرہ کی حمایت میں جو جواب دیا ہے اس کا جواب مصنف نے حاشیہ میں اپنے قول اتمامه على الاشاعرہ ممنوع سے دیا ہے مصنف کہتے ہیں کہ فاضل صاحب کا یہ جواب اشاعرہ کی طرف سے نا تمام اور نا کافی ہے کیونکہ اشاعرہ کے مذہب پر جب بندہ اپنے افعال میں مجبور ہے بندہ کے لئے ارادہ حقیقیہ، اور قدرت حقیقیہ نہیں ہوتی ہے بلکہ قدرت متوهمہ کافی ہے تو بندہ کے لئے ارادہ اعتبار یہ محضہ ہوا اور ارادہ اعتبار یہ بالاستقلال کسی مؤثر کا محتاج نہیں ہوتا لہذا اضطرار لازم نہ آئے گا اس لئے کہ ارادہ اعتبار یہ علت مؤثر ہ کا محتاج نہیں ہے اور جب علت موثرہ نہیں تو وجوب ارادہ نہیں پایا گیا اور جب وجوب ارادہ منتفی ہوا تو وجوب فعل بھی منتفی ہوا اور جب وجوب فعل منتفی ہے تو اضطرا منتفی ہو گیا۔

شبه : ۔ یہاں یہ شبہ کیا جا سکتا ہے کہ اشاعرہ کے نزدیک جب فعل میں ارادہ کو دخل نہیں تو آخر کار انکے نزدیک بندوں کے مکلف ہونے کا سبب کیا ہے؟

جواب :۔ انکے نزدیک مکلف ہونے کے لئے ارادہ اعتبار یہ کافی ہے چنانچہ ان کے نزدیک بندہ حقیقت کے اعتبار سے مجبور ہے اور بظاہر مختار ہے۔ نیز ان کے نزدیک حسن و فتح شرعی ہے پس اگر اللہ عز شانہ مثلاً زید کو ثواب دے اور عمر کو عقاب کرے باوجود یکہ ان دونوں نے مدح و ثواب اور ذم وعقاب کے استحقاق کا کوئی فعل نہیں کیا ہے۔ تو کوئی مضائقہ نہیں چونکہ ان لوگوں کے نزدیک حسن وہ ہے جسکو شارع حسن کہیں اور نتیج وہ ہے جسکو شارع قبیح قرار دیں

قوله " على انه منقوض بفعل البارى تعالى

جواب دوم ۔ درج بالا عبارت میں ماتن نے اشاعرہ کی تیسری دلیل کا دوسرا جواب دیا ہے جواب کا حاصل یہ ھیکہ تمہاری دلیل فعل باری تعالیٰ سے منقوض ہے کیونکہ اسی دلیل سے فعل باری تعالٰی کا اضطراری ہونا لازم آتا ہے وہ اس طرح کہ اللہ تعالیٰ کا فعل ممکن ہے اور ممکن بغیر ترجیح موجود
In both cases, as long as this matter is not realized, intention (irādah) will not be realized, and when intention itself is not realized, the action will not emanate. But when this matter emanates, intention becomes obligatory (wājib), and when intention becomes obligatory, the emanation of action becomes necessary and certain. From the servant, the emanation of action becomes obligatory. So at that time, the obligation of the action from the servant occurs due to that matter over whose existence the servant has no choice; this is called compulsion (iḍṭirār). As for the intervention of intention, it does not harm this compulsion. Rather, that intention strengthens the compulsion, because this intention itself is obligatory and compulsory. Therefore, whatever emanates from it will necessarily be compelled (iḍṭirārī). For that which is voluntary (ikhtiyārī) is that whose performance and abandonment are both permissible, and this action has become so obligatory due to the obligatory intention that its abandonment is impossible. What is impossible to abandon is compelled, and in compulsion there is no good or evil (ḥusn wa quḥ).

Second Exposition: The exposition of the aforementioned scholar's proof can also be given in this manner: All those conditions and prerequisites upon which the existence of the action depends, either all of those prerequisites are realized or not. On the second hypothesis, i.e., if all prerequisites of the action are not realized, then obviously the action will not exist. On the first hypothesis, i.e., if all prerequisites are realized, then the existence of the action becomes necessary and compulsion necessarily follows, because then its abandonment is not permissible. Otherwise (if all prerequisites are found and the existence of the action is not necessary), then preponderance without a preponderator or a contradiction of the hypothesis would necessarily follow, and this would be invalid. Therefore, it must be accepted that when all prerequisites are found, the action exists necessarily and compulsorily. Hence, the author's (Muslim al-Thubut) answer in refutation of the Ash'aris is useless, because in both these proofs, obligation by choice (wujūb bil-ikhtiyār) necessitates compulsion.

Acknowledgment: However, the aforementioned scholar has acknowledged that three objections will inevitably arise here:
(1) There will remain no rational good and evil (ḥusn wa quḥ 'aqlī) in the actions of servants and the actions of Allah.
(2) Allah will not remain absolutely free (mukhtār muṭlaq) in His actions, but He will be a free agent tainted with necessity, as some Mu'tazila and philosophers hold that Allah is a necessitating agent (fā'il mūjib), and the majority of scholastic theologians (mutakallimūn) have opposed this.
(3) It would necessitate that the servant is compelled and forced in his action, thus the judgment of reward and punishment in the Hereafter would become difficult.

His statement in the margin: 'Its completion against the Ash'aris is rejected [mamnū'].'

The author's response to Fadil Mirza Jan's answer in support of the Ash'aris: The author responds in the margin with his statement 'Its completion against the Ash'aris is rejected.' The author says that this answer of the scholar is incomplete and insufficient from the Ash'ari side, because according to the Ash'ari doctrine, when the servant is compelled in his actions, the servant does not have real intention or real power; rather, imagined power (quwwa mutawahhima) suffices. So for the servant, intention is merely conventional (i'tibārī), and conventional intention does not independently need an effective cause. Therefore, compulsion does not necessarily follow, because conventional intention does not need an effective cause, and when there is no effective cause, obligation of intention is not found, and when obligation of intention is negated, obligation of action is also negated, and when obligation of action is negated, compulsion is negated.

Objection: Here it may be objected that according to the Ash'aris, when intention has no role in the action, then ultimately what is the cause of the servants being obligated (mukallaf)?

Answer: According to them, conventional intention suffices for being obligated. Thus, according to them, the servant is compelled in reality and free in appearance. Also, according to them, good and evil are Shar'ī (based on revelation). So if Allah the Exalted, for example, rewards Zayd and punishes 'Umar, even though neither of them performed any action deserving praise and reward or blame and punishment, there is no harm, because according to these people, good is what the Lawgiver calls good and evil is what the Lawgiver declares evil.

His statement: 'Moreover it is refuted by the action of Allah the Exalted.'

Second Answer: In the above passage, the matn (author of Muslim al-Thubut) gives a second answer to the Ash'aris' third proof. The gist of the answer is that your proof is refuted by the action of Allah the Exalted, because by the same proof, the compulsion of Allah's actions necessarily follows. It is as follows: Allah's action is possible, and the possible does not exist without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the Ash'ari position on human action and compulsion (jabr) from a Hanafi perspective. The commentator expands on an argument showing that if all prerequisites for an action are present, the action becomes necessary, leading to compulsion. He then acknowledges three objections to this view: (1) loss of rational good/evil, (2) God's freedom compromised, (3) difficulty in reward/punishment. He responds to a defense by 'Fadil Mirza Jan' by arguing that Ash'ari reliance on conventional intention (irādah i'tibārīyah) avoids the conclusion of compulsion because such intention does not require an effective cause. He also answers the objection that if intention is irrelevant, how can humans be obligated? He says conventional intention suffices, and good/evil are purely Shar'ī. Finally, he offers a second refutation: the same argument would prove that God's actions are compelled, which is absurd, so the Ash'ari argument is flawed.

Key Terms
وجوب بالاختیار (wujūb bil-ikhtiyār): Obligation by choice: the idea that an action becomes necessary (wājib) due to a preceding necessary intention, but the intention itself is necessitated by external factors, resulting in compulsion.
ارادہ اعتباریہ (irādah i'tibārīyah): Conventional intention: a mere attribution of intention to the servant without real causal efficacy, sufficient for Ash'ari taklīf.
حسن و قبح عقلی (ḥusn wa qubḥ 'aqlī): Rational good and evil: moral values that can be known by reason independently of revelation.
اضطرار (iḍṭirār): Compulsion: a state where an action cannot be abandoned because all conditions necessitate it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument presented, what is the difference between an ikhtiyārī (voluntary) action and an iḍṭirārī (compelled) action?
A) Voluntary actions are those performed with intention, while compelled actions are without intention.
B) Voluntary actions allow both performance and abandonment, while compelled actions cannot be abandoned.
C) Voluntary actions are good and compelled actions are evil.
D) Voluntary actions are performed by humans, compelled actions by animals.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi proof establishes that if all prerequisites for an action are present, the action is compelled, and this negates rational good and evil in that action.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced

The Hanafi Refutation of the Ash'ari Defense: Conventional Intention and Its Consequences
This passage refutes the Ash'ari doctrine of compulsion in human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Ash'ari concept of conventional intention (irādah i'tibārīyah) and analyze the Hanafi refutation that it fails to eliminate compulsion, and also evaluate the argument that the same reasoning would compel God's actions.

Core Idea
Conventional intention does not free actions from compulsion; the same proof that shows man's action compelled also compels God's, a conclusion no one accepts.

In the previous lesson, we saw the Hanafi argument that actions become compelled when all prerequisites are present. The Ash'aris, through Fadil Mirza Jan, defended their position by introducing conventional intention and imagined power. In this lesson, we analyze the Hanafi response: conventional intention does not provide real choice, and moreover, the same argument would make God's actions compelled, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Review the Ash'ari defense
Understand the Ash'ari concept: conventional intention (irādah i'tibārīyah) and imagined power (quwwa mutawahhima) do not require an effective cause, so the act is not compelled.
The text says: 'ارادہ اعتبار یہ بالاستقلال کسی مؤثر کا محتاج نہیں ہوتا' (conventional intention does not need an effective cause).
2
Follow the Hanafi rebuttal
The author argues that this defense is incomplete. Conventional intention does not create obligation; the logic of prerequisites still applies because intention, even if conventional, is still a necessary step in the chain.
The author says: 'مصنف کا جواب فاضل مرزا جان نے اشاعرہ کی حمایت میں جو جواب دیا ہے اس کا جواب ... اتمامه على الاشاعرہ ممنوع' (the answer is incomplete and insufficient).
3
Understand the second refutation
The same argument applied to Allah would mean His actions are compelled (since His attributes are prerequisites), which is against the consensus. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The text states: 'تمہاری دلیل فعل باری تعالیٰ سے منقوض ہے کیونکہ اسی دلیل سے فعل باری تعالٰی کا اضطراری ہونا لازم آتا ہے'.
4
Recognize the implications
If the Ash'ari position holds, reward and punishment become arbitrary, as moral values are purely Shar'ī and not rational. The Ash'aris accept this, but the Hanafi consider it problematic.
The passage mentions: 'بندہ حقیقت کے اعتبار سے مجبور ہے اور بظاہر مختار' (the servant is compelled in reality and free in appearance).

بہر دو صورت جیتک یہ امر محقق نہیں ہوگا ارادہ تحقیق نہ ہوگا اور جب ارادہ ہی متحقق نہ ہوگا تو فعل کا صدور نہیں ہو گا۔ البتہ جب اس امر کا صدور ہوگا تو ارادہ واجب ہوگا اور جب ارادہ واجب ہوا تو فعل کا صدور لازم ویقینی ہو البذ ابندہ سے صدور فعل واجب ہوگا پس اسوقت بندہ سے صدور فعل کا وجوب اس امر کے سبب سے ہوا جسکے وجود میں بندہ کو اختیار نہیں ہے اس کا نام اضطرار ہے رہے ارادہ کی مداخلت تو اس اضطرار کو مضر نہیں ۔ بلکہ وہ ارادہ تو اضطرار کو اور پکا کر رہا ہے کیونکہ یہ ارادہ تو خود واجبہ اضطرار یہ ہے لہذا اس سے جو صادر ہوگا وہ ضرور اضطراری ہوگا چونکہ اختیاری وہ ہے جس کا فعل و ترک دونوں جائز ہو اور یہ ایسا فعل ہے جو ارادہ واجبہ کے سبب سے ایسا واجب ہو گیا کہ اسکا ترک ممکن نہیں اور جس کا ترک ممکن نہیں وہ اضطراری ہوتا ہے اور اضطراری میں حسن و فتح نہیں ہوتا۔

دوسری تقریر فاضل مذکور کی دلیل کی تقریر اس انداز میں بھی کر سکتے ہیں۔ کہ تمام وہ شرائط ولوازم جن پر وجود فعل موقوف ہے وہ جملہ موقوف علیہ تحقیق ہیں یا نہیں، بر تقدیر ثانی یعنی فعل کے جملہ موقوف علیہ کا حق نہ ہوا تو ظاہر ہے کہ فعل موجود نہ ہوگا اور بر تقدیر اول یعنی اگر فعل کے تمام موقوف علیہ کا تحقق ہوا تو فعل کا وجود لازم ہوگا اور اضطرار لازم آئے گا کیونکہ اس وقت اسکا ترک جائز نہ ہوگا ورنہ (اگر فعل کے جمیع موقوف علیہ پائے گئے اور وجود فعل لازم نہ ہوا) ترجیح بلا مرج یا خلاف مفروض لازم آئے گا اور یہ باطل اس لئے ماننا پڑے گا کہ جملہ موقوف علیہ پائے جانے کی صورت میں فعل کا وجود لازماً واضطرارا ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا مصنف کا جواب اشاعرہ کے رد میں غیر مفید ہے اس لئے کہ ان دونوں دلیلوں میں وجوب بالاختیار اضطرار کو لازم کر رہا ہے۔

اعتراف : ۔ تاہم جناب فاضل مذکور نے یہ اعتراف کیا ہے کہ یہاں تین اشکالات بہر حال وارد ہوں گے۔

(۱) افعال عباد اور افعال باری تعالٰی میں حسن و فتح عقلی نہیں رہ جائے گا۔

(۲) باری تعالیٰ اپنے افعال میں مختار مطلق نہیں رہ جائے گا بلکہ وہ ایسا مختار ہوگا جو شائبہ
ایجاب سے متصف ہوگا جیسا کہ بعض معتزلہ اور فلاسفہ کا مختار ہے کہ باری تعالیٰ فاعل موجب ہے اور
جمہور متکلمین اس کے خلاف گئے ہیں۔

(۳) لازم آئے گا بندہ کا اپنے فعل میں مضطر مجبور ہونا اسطرح آخرت ومعاد میں ثواب
و عقاب کے مجازات کا حکم دشوار ہو جائے گا۔

قوله فى الحاشية اتمامه على الاشاعره ممنوع

مصنف کا جواب فاضل مرزا جان نے اشاعرہ کی حمایت میں جو جواب دیا ہے اس کا جواب مصنف نے حاشیہ میں اپنے قول اتمامه على الاشاعرہ ممنوع سے دیا ہے مصنف کہتے ہیں کہ فاضل صاحب کا یہ جواب اشاعرہ کی طرف سے نا تمام اور نا کافی ہے کیونکہ اشاعرہ کے مذہب پر جب بندہ اپنے افعال میں مجبور ہے بندہ کے لئے ارادہ حقیقیہ، اور قدرت حقیقیہ نہیں ہوتی ہے بلکہ قدرت متوهمہ کافی ہے تو بندہ کے لئے ارادہ اعتبار یہ محضہ ہوا اور ارادہ اعتبار یہ بالاستقلال کسی مؤثر کا محتاج نہیں ہوتا لہذا اضطرار لازم نہ آئے گا اس لئے کہ ارادہ اعتبار یہ علت مؤثر ہ کا محتاج نہیں ہے اور جب علت موثرہ نہیں تو وجوب ارادہ نہیں پایا گیا اور جب وجوب ارادہ منتفی ہوا تو وجوب فعل بھی منتفی ہوا اور جب وجوب فعل منتفی ہے تو اضطرا منتفی ہو گیا۔

شبه : ۔ یہاں یہ شبہ کیا جا سکتا ہے کہ اشاعرہ کے نزدیک جب فعل میں ارادہ کو دخل نہیں تو آخر کار انکے نزدیک بندوں کے مکلف ہونے کا سبب کیا ہے؟

جواب :۔ انکے نزدیک مکلف ہونے کے لئے ارادہ اعتبار یہ کافی ہے چنانچہ ان کے نزدیک بندہ حقیقت کے اعتبار سے مجبور ہے اور بظاہر مختار ہے۔ نیز ان کے نزدیک حسن و فتح شرعی ہے پس اگر اللہ عز شانہ مثلاً زید کو ثواب دے اور عمر کو عقاب کرے باوجود یکہ ان دونوں نے مدح و ثواب اور ذم وعقاب کے استحقاق کا کوئی فعل نہیں کیا ہے۔ تو کوئی مضائقہ نہیں چونکہ ان لوگوں کے نزدیک حسن وہ ہے جسکو شارع حسن کہیں اور نتیج وہ ہے جسکو شارع قبیح قرار دیں

قوله " على انه منقوض بفعل البارى تعالى

جواب دوم ۔ درج بالا عبارت میں ماتن نے اشاعرہ کی تیسری دلیل کا دوسرا جواب دیا ہے جواب کا حاصل یہ ھیکہ تمہاری دلیل فعل باری تعالیٰ سے منقوض ہے کیونکہ اسی دلیل سے فعل باری تعالٰی کا اضطراری ہونا لازم آتا ہے وہ اس طرح کہ اللہ تعالیٰ کا فعل ممکن ہے اور ممکن بغیر ترجیح موجود
In both cases, as long as this matter is not realized, intention (irādah) will not be realized, and when intention itself is not realized, the action will not emanate. But when this matter emanates, intention becomes obligatory (wājib), and when intention becomes obligatory, the emanation of action becomes necessary and certain. From the servant, the emanation of action becomes obligatory. So at that time, the obligation of the action from the servant occurs due to that matter over whose existence the servant has no choice; this is called compulsion (iḍṭirār). As for the intervention of intention, it does not harm this compulsion. Rather, that intention strengthens the compulsion, because this intention itself is obligatory and compulsory. Therefore, whatever emanates from it will necessarily be compelled (iḍṭirārī). For that which is voluntary (ikhtiyārī) is that whose performance and abandonment are both permissible, and this action has become so obligatory due to the obligatory intention that its abandonment is impossible. What is impossible to abandon is compelled, and in compulsion there is no good or evil (ḥusn wa quḥ).

Second Exposition: The exposition of the aforementioned scholar's proof can also be given in this manner: All those conditions and prerequisites upon which the existence of the action depends, either all of those prerequisites are realized or not. On the second hypothesis, i.e., if all prerequisites of the action are not realized, then obviously the action will not exist. On the first hypothesis, i.e., if all prerequisites are realized, then the existence of the action becomes necessary and compulsion necessarily follows, because then its abandonment is not permissible. Otherwise (if all prerequisites are found and the existence of the action is not necessary), then preponderance without a preponderator or a contradiction of the hypothesis would necessarily follow, and this would be invalid. Therefore, it must be accepted that when all prerequisites are found, the action exists necessarily and compulsorily. Hence, the author's (Muslim al-Thubut) answer in refutation of the Ash'aris is useless, because in both these proofs, obligation by choice (wujūb bil-ikhtiyār) necessitates compulsion.

Acknowledgment: However, the aforementioned scholar has acknowledged that three objections will inevitably arise here:
(1) There will remain no rational good and evil (ḥusn wa quḥ 'aqlī) in the actions of servants and the actions of Allah.
(2) Allah will not remain absolutely free (mukhtār muṭlaq) in His actions, but He will be a free agent tainted with necessity, as some Mu'tazila and philosophers hold that Allah is a necessitating agent (fā'il mūjib), and the majority of scholastic theologians (mutakallimūn) have opposed this.
(3) It would necessitate that the servant is compelled and forced in his action, thus the judgment of reward and punishment in the Hereafter would become difficult.

His statement in the margin: 'Its completion against the Ash'aris is rejected [mamnū'].'

The author's response to Fadil Mirza Jan's answer in support of the Ash'aris: The author responds in the margin with his statement 'Its completion against the Ash'aris is rejected.' The author says that this answer of the scholar is incomplete and insufficient from the Ash'ari side, because according to the Ash'ari doctrine, when the servant is compelled in his actions, the servant does not have real intention or real power; rather, imagined power (quwwa mutawahhima) suffices. So for the servant, intention is merely conventional (i'tibārī), and conventional intention does not independently need an effective cause. Therefore, compulsion does not necessarily follow, because conventional intention does not need an effective cause, and when there is no effective cause, obligation of intention is not found, and when obligation of intention is negated, obligation of action is also negated, and when obligation of action is negated, compulsion is negated.

Objection: Here it may be objected that according to the Ash'aris, when intention has no role in the action, then ultimately what is the cause of the servants being obligated (mukallaf)?

Answer: According to them, conventional intention suffices for being obligated. Thus, according to them, the servant is compelled in reality and free in appearance. Also, according to them, good and evil are Shar'ī (based on revelation). So if Allah the Exalted, for example, rewards Zayd and punishes 'Umar, even though neither of them performed any action deserving praise and reward or blame and punishment, there is no harm, because according to these people, good is what the Lawgiver calls good and evil is what the Lawgiver declares evil.

His statement: 'Moreover it is refuted by the action of Allah the Exalted.'

Second Answer: In the above passage, the matn (author of Muslim al-Thubut) gives a second answer to the Ash'aris' third proof. The gist of the answer is that your proof is refuted by the action of Allah the Exalted, because by the same proof, the compulsion of Allah's actions necessarily follows. It is as follows: Allah's action is possible, and the possible does not exist without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the Ash'ari position on human action and compulsion (jabr) from a Hanafi perspective. The commentator expands on an argument showing that if all prerequisites for an action are present, the action becomes necessary, leading to compulsion. He then acknowledges three objections to this view: (1) loss of rational good/evil, (2) God's freedom compromised, (3) difficulty in reward/punishment. He responds to a defense by 'Fadil Mirza Jan' by arguing that Ash'ari reliance on conventional intention (irādah i'tibārīyah) avoids the conclusion of compulsion because such intention does not require an effective cause. He also answers the objection that if intention is irrelevant, how can humans be obligated? He says conventional intention suffices, and good/evil are purely Shar'ī. Finally, he offers a second refutation: the same argument would prove that God's actions are compelled, which is absurd, so the Ash'ari argument is flawed.

Key Terms
وجوب بالاختیار (wujūb bil-ikhtiyār): Obligation by choice: the idea that an action becomes necessary (wājib) due to a preceding necessary intention, but the intention itself is necessitated by external factors, resulting in compulsion.
ارادہ اعتباریہ (irādah i'tibārīyah): Conventional intention: a mere attribution of intention to the servant without real causal efficacy, sufficient for Ash'ari taklīf.
حسن و قبح عقلی (ḥusn wa qubḥ 'aqlī): Rational good and evil: moral values that can be known by reason independently of revelation.
اضطرار (iḍṭirār): Compulsion: a state where an action cannot be abandoned because all conditions necessitate it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the second refutation of the Ash'ari position?
A) The argument is refuted by the action of Allah (God's actions would be compelled if the Ash'ari logic were applied).
B) Conventional intention is impossible.
C) Humans have no intention at all.
D) The argument is accepted.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari concept of conventional intention fails to remove compulsion, as the Hanafi proof from prerequisites still applies, and the same reasoning would compel God's actions.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Obligation of Nazar: Ash'ari vs Hanafi Dispute
Dispute between Hanafis and Ash'aris on the obligation of reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Ash'ari argument for ihtimal al-wujub and identify the Hanafi response regarding the role of reason in shar'i rulings.

Core Idea
Probability of obligation does not create a legal obligation if the obligation of compliance is itself legal, not rational.

In this passage, the author engages in a classic usul dispute with the Ash'aris over the obligation of reflection (nazar) to know God. The Ash'aris argue that the mere probability of obligation (ihtimal al-wujub) is sufficient to necessitate nazar to avoid potential harm. The Hanafi response distinguishes between rational and legal obligations, showing that probability, being a rational judgment, cannot intervene in a purely legal (shar'i) ruling. The dispute reveals deeper differences about the basis of taklif (legal responsibility).

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Determine what exactly is being disputed: whether ihtimal al-wujub suffices to make nazar wajib.
The issue arises from a Mu'tazila argument about obligation.
2
Understand the Hanafi position
Note that Hanafis hold nazar as rationally obligatory (wajib aqliyyan) independent of sharia, and that if wujub al-imtithal is shar'i, ihtimal cannot create obligation.
This avoids a logical loop.
3
Analyze the Ash'ari argument
The Ash'aris claim probability of obligation is enough to necessitate nazar to avoid potential harm, citing human caution.
They argue that a mukallaf must reflect to be safe.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The author refutes that probability is a rational judgment; if the ruling is shar'i, reason cannot intervene. Thus ihtimal is insufficient.
This forces the Ash'aris into a dilemma.
5
Consider the second Ash'ari argument and resolution
The Ash'aris appeal to human nature (e.g., looking behind if warned of a beast). The Hanafis accept this as rational necessity, but still see it as distinct from legal obligation.
The Hanafi position remains that nazar is wajib aqliyyan, not shar'iyyan.

اوں ، تو مکلف کا یہ قول انجام نبی کے لئے محل جواز بن سکتا ہے کہ اسطرح دلیل کی تقریر کرنے سے نبی کا اسکات و بجز لازم آئے گا اور معتزلہ کی دلیل مذکور کے خلاف ماسبق والا جواب بھی کوئی نفع بخش نہ ہوگیا کیونکہ وجوب امتثال اگر عقلی یعنی عقل سے ثابت ہے تو ظاہر ہے کہ نظری ہوگا تو اس صورت میں وجوب امتثال ( نظری ہونے کے سبب ) نظر پر موقوف ہوگا نہ کہ امتثال بالشرع پر ۔ اور جب وجوب امتثال امتثال بالشرع پر موقوف نہیں ہے تو اس تقدیر پر انجام رسول لازم نہ آئے گا جیسا کہ ماسبق میں گذرا۔ البتہ اگر وجوب امتثال عقلی نہیں بلکہ شرعی یعنی شرع سے ثابت ہوتو وجوب امتثال ، امتثال پر موقوف ہوگا اس لئے کہ وجوب امتثال کا علم شرعی ہونے کی تقدیر پر امتثال بالشرع پر موقوف ہونا بد یہی ہے پس اس تقریر پر انعام رسول لازم آئے گا جیسا کہ مصنف کی اس دلیل میں مذکور ہے۔
اس مقام پر مصنف نے حاشیہ آرائی کی ہے ملاحظہ ہو۔
ان قيل ان احتمال الوجوب كاف لوجوب النظر دفعاً لاحتمال الضرر في المأل وللخوف فى الحال قلنا ولوسلم ذالك فهو من قضية العقل واذا كان الحكم شرعياً كما هو مذهبكم لايكون فيه مداخلة واما حكم الشرع فلم يعلم بعد فلعل حكم العقل هدرهناک ولوقيل ان الجبلة الانسانية خلقت على انها لوقال احد خلفك السبع ينظر البتة الى عقبه وانكار ذالک عناد و مكابرة يقال ذالك احسن ما يقال فى هذا المقام لكنه يعود الى الجواب الحق انتهت
حاشیہ مندرجہ میں اشاعرہ کی طرف سے اس نظر کا جواب ہے جو ماتن نے معتزلہ کی دلیل کی توضیح میں کہا ہے۔ جسکا بیان ابھی گذرا۔
جواب :۔ مصنف کی ذکر کردہ دلیل پر اشاعرہ کہتے ہیں کہ مکلف اگر چہ وجوب کے عدم علم کے اعتبار سے حجت قائم کر سکتا ہے تا ہم وجوب امتثال امر نظر پر موقوف نہیں ہے بلکہ احتمال وجوب کافی ہے پس اس وقت نبی کے لئے جائز ہے کہ وہ کہیں کہ احتمال وجوب موجود متحقق ہے لہذا تم مکلف پر نظر کرنا واجب ہے تا کہ تم عدم امتثال کے سبب ہلاکت و بر بادی میں نہ پڑو کیونکہ مخلمندوں کی یہ شان نہیں ہے کہ وہ مستقبل کی ہلاکت خیز اور ضرر رساں چیزوں کے احتمال سے اور حال و حاضر کے خوف کے احتمال سے نہ بچے۔ مصنف اشاعرہ کے خیال کا رد کرتے ہوئے کہتے ہیں کہ ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے ہیں کہ احتمال وجوب، وجوب نظر کے لئے کافی ہے اور اگر تسلیم کر لیتے ہیں پھر بھی تم اشاعرہ کے لئے مفید و کافی نہیں اسلئے کہ احتمال وجوب کا تحقق قضیہ عقل سے ہے اور جب حکم تمہارے نزدیک حکم شرعی ہے تو عقل کو اس میں کیونکر مداخلت ہوگی لہذا اس صورت میں علم وجوب کے لئے سوائے امتثال امر شارع کے کوئی اور چارہ نہیں رہ جاتا ہے تو مکلف شارع پر حجت قائم کر لے گا اور اس طرح انجام رسول لازم آئے گا۔ قوله فى الحاشية " ولوقيل ان الجبلة الانسانيه الخ
مصنف کی دلیل کا اشاعرہ کی طرف سے دوسرا جواب ہے۔
اس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ نبی کے معجزہ کا حکم دینے پر مکلف کا یہ کہنا انظر ولا امتثل مالم اعلم الوجوب تعنت وعناد پر مبنی ہے۔ اور راہ حق وصواب سے کجروی کی طرف مقتضی ہے اس لئے کہ وجوب وغیرہ جملہ احکام اگر چہ اس تقدیر پر شرعی ہیں مگر اشاعرہ وحنفیہ مصالح ومضار اور صفت کمال ونقصان کا عقلی طور پر اور اک کئے جانے کو انکار نہیں کرتے کہ حسن وفتح کا یہ دونوں معنی بالا تفاق عقلی ہے پس اشاعرہ و حنفیہ کے نزدیک بھی عقل اتنی سی بات کا بخوبی ادراک کر سکتی ہے کہ وہ مخبر صادق جن کے احوال و آثار سے صدق عیاں اور ظاہر ہے مضرت ابدیہ سے احتراز کی خبر دے رہے ہیں تو ایسے میں ہر عاقل کے لئے ضروری ہے کہ ان کے کلام میں اور ان چیزوں میں جو انکی صداقت پر دال ہونظر کرے پھر اس کی صداقت وحقانیت واضح ہو جانے کے بعد مضرت ابدیہ کے دفعیہ کی کوشش کرے اگر مکلف ایسے مخبر صادق کی خبر پر دھیان نہیں دیتا ہے اور یہ کہتے ہوئے راہ فرار اختیار کرلے کہ میں تمہارے کلام میں نظر نہ کرونگا جب تک مجھ پر تمہاری اتباع لازم نہ ہو گی تو ایسے شخص کو اہل عقل، عناد و حماقت کی طرف منسوب کرتے ہیں اس لئے کہ ہر عاقل پر لازم ہے کہ مصلحت کو طلب کرے اور مضرت سے احتراز کرے بلکہ جبلت انسانیہ اس پر پیدا ہی کی گئی ہے کیا نہیں دیکھتے کہ اگر کسی شخص نے کسی سے کہا کہ درندہ تمہارے پیچھے ہے تو وہ شخص اپنے پیچھے ضرور نظر کرتا ہے جب کہ کہنے والا عام شخص ہوتا ہے اور دنیا کی ادنی ہلاکت کے احتمالی سبب کے بارے میں خبر دے رہا ہے تو ہر شخص اس درندہ سے بچنے کی فکر کرتا ہے تو تمہارا کیا خیال ہے اس شخص کی خبر کے بارے میں جس کا صدق ظاہر وواضح ہے وہ بھی ایسی خبر دے
So, the Mukallaf's saying can become a place of permissibility for the Prophet's conclusion, because by presenting the proof in this way, the Prophet's silence and... become necessary, and the previous response against the Mu'tazila's mentioned proof also became useless. Because if the obligation of compliance is rational, i.e., established by reason, then it is evident that it will be theoretical, so in that case the obligation of compliance (being theoretical) depends on reflection, not on compliance by Sharia. And when the obligation of compliance does not depend on compliance by Sharia, then on this estimation the Prophet's conclusion will not be necessary, as passed earlier. But if the obligation of compliance is not rational but rather legal, i.e., established by Sharia, then the obligation of compliance depends on compliance, because the knowledge of the obligation of compliance, on the estimation of it being legal, is self-evident that it depends on compliance by Sharia. So on this estimation, the Prophet's conclusion will be necessary, as mentioned in the author's proof. At this point the author has written a gloss, please observe: 'If it is said that probability of obligation is sufficient for the obligation of reflection to avert the probability of harm in the future and fear in the present, we say: Even if we accept that, it is from the judgment of reason, and when the ruling is legal as per your doctrine, reason has no intervention in it. As for the ruling of Sharia, it is not yet known, so perhaps the judgment of reason is null here. And if it is said that human nature is created such that if someone says behind you is a beast, he definitely looks at his heel, and denying that is stubbornness and obstinacy, it is said that is the best that can be said in this context, but it returns to the true response. End.' The included gloss is the response from the Ash'aris to the reflection that the Matan said in explanation of the Mu'tazila's proof, whose statement has just passed. Response: On the author's mentioned proof, the Ash'aris say that even though the Mukallaf can establish an argument based on non-knowledge of obligation, still the obligation of compliance does not depend on the command of reflection; rather, probability of obligation is sufficient. So at that time it is permissible for the Prophet to say that probability of obligation exists and is realized, therefore it is obligatory upon you, Mukallaf, to reflect, so that you do not fall into destruction and ruin due to non-compliance, because it is not the nature of cautious people to avoid the probability of future harmful and damaging things and the probability of present fear. The author, refuting the Ash'ari opinion, says: We do not accept that probability of obligation is sufficient for the obligation of reflection, and even if we accept, it is still not useful and sufficient for you Ash'aris, because the realization of probability of obligation is from the judgment of reason, and when the ruling is legal according to you, how can reason intervene in it? So in this case, for knowledge of obligation, there is no way except compliance with the command of the Lawgiver, so the Mukallaf will establish an argument against the Lawgiver, and thus the Prophet's conclusion will be necessary. His saying in the gloss: 'And if it is said that human nature...' is the second response from the Ash'aris to the author's proof. Its gist is that upon the Prophet's giving the judgment of miracle, the Mukallaf's saying 'I reflect but do not comply until I know the obligation' is based on obstinacy and stubbornness, and leads to deviation from the path of truth and righteousness, because even though all rulings like obligation are legal on this estimation, yet the Ash'aris and Hanafis do not deny that benefits and harms and the attribute of perfection and deficiency are rationally known, for these two meanings of beauty and ugliness are by agreement rational. So according to the Ash'aris and Hanafis, reason can very well perceive this much: that a truthful informer whose truthfulness is evident and manifest from his states and signs, gives news of avoiding eternal harm, so in such a case it is necessary for every rational person to reflect on his words and on those things that indicate his truthfulness, then after his truthfulness and rightness become clear, to strive for the repulsion of eternal harm. If the Mukallaf does not pay attention to such a truthful informer's news and takes the path of escape by saying 'I will not reflect on your words until compliance with you becomes obligatory on me,' then people of reason attribute such a person to stubbornness and foolishness, because it is necessary for every rational person to seek benefit and avoid harm. Rather, human nature is created upon this: do you not see that if someone says to another that a beast is behind you, that person definitely looks behind him, even though the speaker is an ordinary person and is giving news about a probable cause of minimal worldly destruction, then every person thinks about escaping from that beast. So what is your opinion about the news of that person whose truthfulness is apparent and evident, and he gives such news?
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute regarding the obligation of nazar (reflection) to know God. The author (a Hanafi commentator) engages with the Ash'ari position that ihtimal al-wujub (probability of obligation) is sufficient to necessitate nazar, in response to a Mu'tazila argument. The core argument: if wujub al-imtithal (obligation of compliance) is rational, then it depends on nazar, so the prophet cannot force compliance; if it is legal (shar'i), then compliance depends on the prophet's command, and the mukallaf can argue that he doesn't know obligation, leading to a loop. The Ash'aris propose ihtimal al-wujub as a solution. The author refutes: even if ihtimal al-wujub is accepted, it is a rational judgment, but according to Ash'aris the rulings are shar'i, so reason cannot intervene. Then a second Ash'ari argument based on human nature: a rational person naturally avoids harm; a prophet warning of eternal harm must be heeded. The author responds that this rational necessity is independent of legal obligation. The Hanafi position is that nazar is wajib aqliyyan (rationally obligatory) even without prior knowledge of sharia.

Key Terms
وجوب امتثال (wujub al-imtithal): The obligation of compliance with a command; the text discusses whether it is rational (aqli) or legal (shar'i).
احتمال وجوب (ihtimal al-wujub): Probability of obligation; the Ash'aris argue it is sufficient to necessitate nazar to avoid potential harm.
نظر (nazar): Reflection or intellectual inquiry to know God and His commands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is ihtimal al-wujub (probability of obligation) not sufficient to necessitate nazar if wujub al-imtithal is shar'i?
A) Because the Mukallaf already knows the obligation
B) Because probability is a rational judgment, and reason cannot intervene in a shar'i ruling
C) Because the Prophet's command is always clear
D) Because the Ash'aris have no proof

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that rational necessity compels reflection (nazar) independently of any legal command, and mere probability of obligation cannot create a legal duty if obligation is purely legal.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Priority of Wadʿ over Iqtiḍā' in Hanafi Usul
Analysing an objection and response in Hanafi usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn al-Humām's objection regarding the ontological priority of wadʿ over iqtiḍā' and evaluate the Hanafi response that both are necessary in the definition of ḥukm.

Core Idea
Wadʿ (causation) is ontologically prior to iqtiḍā' (implication); thus both are needed in the definition of ḥukm.

In Hanafi uṣūl, a central question is whether the definition of ḥukm must include both wadʿ (the Lawgiver's stipulation of causes) and iqtiḍā' (the implication of rulings). Some scholars argued that iqtiḍā' is general enough to encompass wadʿ, making a separate mention redundant. Ibn al-Humām of al-Taḥrīr objected, pointing out that wadʿ is ontologically prior to iqtiḍā'—causes exist before their effects. This lesson unpacks that objection and the commentator's response, clarifying why both components are indispensable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Definitional Element
Recognize that the dispute concerns whether the definition of ḥukm should include 'wadʿ' or whether 'iqtiḍā' alone suffices. Those who omit wadʿ claim iqtiḍā' implicitly covers it.
The passage refers to those who did not add 'wadʿ' in the definition.
2
State Ibn al-Humām's Objection
Ibn al-Humām argues that wadʿ is prior in existence to iqtiḍā'. For example, the Lawgiver first establishes the cause (sabab) like the declination of the sun for prayer, and only then does the ruling of obligation (iqtiḍā') follow. Since the cause precedes its effect, wadʿ cannot be subsumed under iqtiḍā'.
The objection is ontological: wadʿ is prior, iqtiḍā' is posterior.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The commentator (Māyīn) responds that the objection does not harm the earlier position. The reason is that even though wadʿ is prior, iqtiḍā' includes both explicit and implicit implications, and the implicit covers the cause-effect relationship. However, the passage implies that both terms are still needed for completeness—the objection does not invalidate the separate mention.
The response is not a rejection but a refinement: the priority does not cause a problem.
4
Derive the Usul Principle
From this discussion, the principle is that in defining ḥukm, both wadʿ and iqtiḍā' are necessary because they represent different aspects: wadʿ captures the Lawgiver's act of establishing causes (priority), while iqtiḍā' captures the resultant ruling (posterior). Omitting wadʿ would lose the causal dimension.
This principle affects how we understand the structure of rulings in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشیہ میں مائن نے کہا ہے ۔
"ثم لا يرد القصص على ما وهم فانها من حيث هي خبر لايدل على الحـ
وانما يفهم من مثل قوله فاعتبروا انتهت
یعنی قصص سے اعتراض نہ پڑے گا جیسا کہ وہم کیا گیا ہے کیونکہ قصہ من حیث ھی خبر ہے جس کی دلالت حکم پر نہیں ہوتی البتہ حکم اس کے قول فاعتبر واسے مفہوم ہوتا ہے۔ قوله ومافى التحرير أن الوضع مقدم عليه لايضر لصدق الخ ماسبق سے یہ معلوم ہو چکا ہے کہ حکم کی تعریف میں جن لوگوں نے وضع کی قید کا اضافہ نہیں کیا ہے وہ احکام وصیغہ کے خروج سے بچنے کے لئے یہ کہتے ہیں کہ اقتضاء کے ذکر کے بعد وضع کے ذکر کی حاجت نہیں رہجاتی ، اس لئے کہ اقتضاء صریحی و ضمنی سے عام ہے یعنی وضع ، اقتضاء میں داخل ہے۔ اسپر امام ابن الحمام صاحب تحریر کو اعتراض ہے ۔ مائن نے درج بالا عبارت میں تحریر میں ذکر اعتراض کی طرف اشارہ کرتے ہوئے اس کا جواب دیا ہے۔ اعتراض : ۔ یہ ہے کہ وضع تحقق ووجود کے اعتبار سے مقدم ہوتا ہے اور اقتضاء وضع سے مؤخر ہوتا ہے مثال کے طور پر یہ مجھو کہ شارع نے سبب صلوۃ مثلاً دلوک الشمس کو وجوب نماز کا موجب قرار دیا ہے اور موجب مقدم ہوتا ہے۔ بلفظ دیگر یہ کہیں کہ وقت ، وجوب صلوٰۃ کیلئے سبب ہے اور وجوب صلوٰۃ کا حکم مسبب ہے اور یہ ظاہر ہے کہ سبب مقدم ہوتا ہے اور مسبب مؤخر ہوتا ہے لہذا وضع بہر حال اقتضاء تکلیفی.
In the margin, the author said: 'Then stories are not brought as objection as was imagined, for they, as news, do not indicate a ruling; rather the ruling is understood from such statements as "So take heed" (end).' Meaning: The objection of stories will not occur as was imagined because a story as such is news and does not indicate a ruling; rather the ruling is understood from His statement 'So take heed'. His statement: 'And what is in al-Taḥrīr that wadʿ is prior to it does not harm because... etc.' From the preceding it is already known that those who did not add the restriction of wadʿ in the definition of ruling say, to avoid the exclusion of wadʿ rulings and ṣīgha rulings, that after mentioning iqtiḍā' there is no need to mention wadʿ because iqtiḍā' is general encompassing explicit and implicit, meaning wadʿ is included in iqtiḍā'. There is an objection to this from Imam Ibn al-Humam, author of al-Taḥrīr. In the above phrase, the author alluded to the objection in al-Taḥrīr and answered it. The objection is that wadʿ is prior in terms of actualization and existence, while iqtiḍā' is posterior to wadʿ. For example, it is established that the Lawgiver made the cause of prayer, such as the declination of the sun, a necessitating factor for the obligation of prayer, and the necessitating factor is prior. In other words, time is the cause for the obligation of prayer, and the ruling of obligation is the effect, and it is clear that the cause is prior and the effect is posterior. Thus wadʿ is definitely prior to iqtiḍā' taklīfī.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage resolves a subtle objection in Hanafi usul: if iqtiḍā' is general enough to include wadʿ (stipulation/cause), why mention wadʿ separately in the definition of ḥukm? Ibn al-Humām argues that wadʿ is ontologically prior to iqtiḍā' because causes (sabab) precede their effects. For example, the time of prayer (cause) is established before the obligation (effect) arises. Thus wadʿ cannot be subsumed under iqtiḍā' without losing this priority. The commentator (Māyīn) states that this does not harm the earlier position, implying that both are needed for a complete definition.

Key Terms
وضع (wadʿ): Stipulation or cause; the act of the Lawgiver in establishing a cause (sabab) that brings about a ruling. It is ontologically prior to the ruling.
اقتضاء (iqtiḍā'): The implication of a ruling from a text or cause; it is posterior to wadʿ because it is derived after the cause is established.
سبب (sabab): The cause that leads to a ruling, e.g., the time of prayer causing obligation. It is prior in existence to the ruling.
مسبب (musabbab): The effect, i.e., the ruling that results from the cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis of Ibn al-Humām's objection to those who omit 'wadʿ' from the definition of ḥukm?
A) Wadʿ is more specific than iqtiḍā'.
B) Wadʿ is ontologically prior to iqtiḍā'—causes precede their effects.
C) Wadʿ is a type of iqtiḍā' that cannot be omitted.
D) Iqtiḍā' is prior to wadʿ in existence.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the definition of ḥukm must include both wadʿ (stipulation of causes) and iqtiḍā' (implication) because causes are ontologically prior and cannot be subsumed under implication alone.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

8 min
Clarifying Hanafi Position: Qiyas as Mazhar par Excellence
Resolves objection that Hanafis consider only qiyas as mazhar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis describe Qiyas as mazhar (manifestor) while still affirming Sunnah and Ijma as mazhar, and evaluate the objection based on apparent wording.

Core Idea
Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas are all mazhar; Qiyas is called mazhar more clearly due to its explicit far'iyyah.

This passage addresses a potential contradiction: earlier it was stated that all three principles (Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) are kashif/mazhar of the divine address, but some Hanafis have said that only Qiyas is mazhar. The objection is that if all three are kashif, why single out Qiyas? The response clarifies that those Hanafis do accept Sunnah and Ijma as kashif, but they reserve the term 'kashif' specifically for Qiyas because Qiyas is more clearly a branch (far') of the divine address. Understanding this nuance prevents confusion about the Hanafi hierarchy of sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Recognize the apparent contradiction: the author earlier stated all three sources are kashif, but some Hanafis say only Qiyas is kashif. State the objection clearly in your own words.
The objection is based on the wording of some Hanafi texts.
2
Affirm the Hanafi position
State the correct Hanafi position: all three sources (Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) are mazhar/kashif of the divine address. Find the evidence in the response that confirms this.
The response explicitly says 'they also consider Sunnah and Ijma as kashif'.
3
Analyze the reasoning for emphasis
Understand why some Hanafis emphasize Qiyas as mazhar: because Qiyas is more explicit in being a branch (far') of the divine address. Qiyas requires an asl and illah, making its subordination to the original address more transparent.
Sunnah and Ijma derive rulings directly without needing an external original, so their branch-status is less obvious.
4
Compare the mechanisms
Contrast the derivation process of Qiyas versus Sunnah/Ijma. For Qiyas, one must identify an asl and illah to transfer the ruling. For Sunnah and Ijma, the ruling is derived directly from the text or consensus without additional steps.
This difference in procedure is why Qiyas is called 'mazhar' more explicitly.
5
Draw the conclusion: thamarah al-khilaf
Explain the practical difference: there is no change in the classification of sources; all four (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) remain. The difference is only in the clarity of their derivative nature. Therefore, the objection is resolved.
This is a dispute in terminology, not in substance.

قوله - وماعن الحنيفة أن القياس مظهر بخلاف السنة والاجماع درجہ بالا عبارت میں ایک اعتراض کی طرف اشارہ ہے۔ اعتراض :- یہ ہے کہ آپ کی تقریر بالا سے واضح ہیکہ سنت واجماع اور قیاس تینوں اصول خطاب کیلئے کاشف ہیں حالانکہ اسکے برخلاف بعض حنفیہ کے کلام سے پتہ چلتا ہے کہ خطاب کیلئے کاشف صرف قیاس ہے رہے سنت واجماع تو وہ دونوں کا شف نہیں بلکہ مثبت ہیں۔ پس جب کاشف تینوں ہیں تو بعض حنفیہ نے صرف قیاس ہی کو کیوں کا شف قرار دیا۔ قوله فمبنى على انه اصرح فى الفرعية فتأمل. اس عبارت سے بعض حنفیہ پر جو اعتراض وارد ہے اسکے جواب کی طرف اشارہ ہے۔ جواب:۔ یہ صحیح ہے کہ بعض حنفیہ سے منقول ہیکہ صرف قیاس کاشف و مظہر الا حکام ہے مگر ان کی یہ مراد ہر گز نہیں ہے کہ صرف قیاس ہی کاشف ہے اور سنت و اجماع کاشف نہیں ہیں۔ بلکہ وہ حضرات قیاس کے ساتھ ساتھ سنت و اجماع کو بھی خطاب شرعی کا کاشف و مظہر مانتے ہیں۔ البتہ صرف قیاس پر کاشف کا اس لئے اطلاق کرتے ہیں کہ سنت و اجماع کے بالمقابل خطاب شرعی کی فرعیت میں قیاس زیادہ صریح اور واضح ہے چونکہ قیاس میں استنباط حکم کے وقت ایک اصل (مقیس علیہ ) کی ضرورت پڑتی ہے تا کہ مقیس علیہ کے حکم کی علت کو سامنے رکھکر فرع و مقیس کی علت کے اشتراک کی تقدیر پر مقیس علیہ کے حکم کا مقیس میں تعدیہ کیا جاسکے ۔ اسکے برعکس سنت واجماع سے اخذ حکم کیلئے ان دونوں کے علاوہ مزید کسی امر آخر کی ضرورت نہیں پڑتی۔ اگر چہ یہ دونوں بھی خطاب از لی شرعی کیلئے کاشف ہیں اور اسکے فرع ہی ہیں مگر قیاس کے بہ نسبت ان دونوں کی فرعیت اخف واخفی ہے۔
His statement: 'And as for what is from the Hanafis that qiyas is mazhar unlike the Sunnah and Ijma.' In the above phrase, an objection is indicated. The objection: It is clear from your foregoing exposition that the three principles — Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas — are kashif for the address. However, contrary to this, from the statement of some Hanafis it appears that the kashif for the address is only qiyas; as for Sunnah and Ijma, they are not kashif but positive proofs (muthbit). So when all three are kashif, why did some Hanafis consider only qiyas as kashif? His statement: 'It is built upon the fact that it is more explicit in being a branch; consider carefully.' By this phrase, the answer to the objection that arose against some Hanafis is indicated. The answer: It is true that it is reported from some Hanafis that only qiyas is kashif and mazhar of rulings. But their intention is never that only qiyas is kashif and Sunnah and Ijma are not kashif. Rather, those scholars, along with qiyas, also consider Sunnah and Ijma as kashif and mazhar of the Shariah address. However, they apply the term kashif specifically to qiyas because, compared to Sunnah and Ijma, qiyas is more explicit and clear in being a branch (far') of the Shariah address. This is because in qiyas, at the time of deriving a ruling, an original (maqis 'alayhi) is needed so that, by considering the illah of the original's ruling, the transference of the original's ruling to the branch (maqis) can be effected based on the estimated sharing of the illah. In contrast, for deriving rulings from Sunnah and Ijma, no additional thing beyond them is needed. Although these two are also kashif for the Shariah address and are its branches, yet in comparison to qiyas, their being branches is more hidden and concealed.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential contradiction: earlier it was stated that all three principles (Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) are kashif/mazhar of the divine address, but some Hanafis have said that only Qiyas is mazhar. The objection is that if all three are kashif, why single out Qiyas? The response clarifies that those Hanafis do accept Sunnah and Ijma as kashif, but they reserve the term 'kashif' specifically for Qiyas because Qiyas is more clearly a branch (far') of the divine address. The reasoning is that Qiyas explicitly requires an original (asl) and an illah to transfer the ruling, making its subordination to the divine address more apparent. In contrast, Sunnah and Ijma derive rulings directly without needing an external original, so their branch-status is less obvious, though they are indeed branches. Thus, the Hanafi position is consistent: all three are mazhar, but calling Qiyas 'kashif' par excellence is due to its more explicit far'iyyah.

Key Terms
مظہر (mazhar): A manifestor or disclosure; here, a source that manifests the divine address and its rulings.
فرعیت (far'iyyah): The quality of being a branch or derivative; subordination to an original source.
اصل (asl): The original source or basis in Qiyas; the maqis 'alayh from which the ruling is transferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do some Hanafis say only Qiyas is mazhar when all three (Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) are sources?
A) Because Sunnah and Ijma are not actually sources.
B) Because Qiyas is more clearly a branch (far') of the divine address.
C) Because Qiyas is independent of the divine address.
D) Because Sunnah and Ijma are not kashif.

Key Takeaway
Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas are all mazhar; calling Qiyas 'kashif' emphasizes its more explicit branch-status due to requirement of asl and illah.

Qiyas as the Primary Manifestor: A Terminological Distinction
Clarifying an objection about qiyas being the only revealer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why some Hanafis reserve the term 'kashif' for qiyas and why this does not diminish the status of sunnah and ijma.

Core Idea
Qiyas is called 'kashif' because it is more explicitly a branch of divine address, but sunnah and ijma are equally valid revealers.

This passage addresses a potential objection: if sunnah, ijma, and qiyas all yield rulings, why do some Hanafis call only qiyas a 'kashif'? The answer reveals an important nuance about how these sources relate to the divine address—it is a matter of terminological emphasis, not substantive denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Recognize the claim that some Hanafis restrict 'kashif' to qiyas, seemingly excluding sunnah and ijma.
The objection is based on a literal reading of their statements.
2
Understand the response
The commentator clarifies that all three are kashif, but qiyas is more explicit in its branch-hood (far'iyya).
Branch-hood means deriving a ruling from an original source.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Qiyas requires an original case (asl) and a shared cause ('illa), making its derivative nature explicit. Sunnah and ijma also derive from the divine address but do not require an external asl.
This does not imply that sunnah and ijma are less authoritative.
4
Confirm the practical outcome
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is none; all three sources are accepted for deriving rulings.
The dispute is purely terminological.

قوله - وماعن الحنيفة أن القياس مظهر بخلاف السنة والاجماع درجہ بالا عبارت میں ایک اعتراض کی طرف اشارہ ہے۔
اعتراض :- یہ ہے کہ آپ کی تقریر بالا سے واضح ہیکہ سنت واجماع اور قیاس تینوں اصول خطاب کیلئے کاشف ہیں حالانکہ اسکے برخلاف بعض حنفیہ کے کلام سے پتہ چلتا ہے کہ خطاب کیلئے کاشف صرف قیاس ہے رہے سنت واجماع تو وہ دونوں کا شف نہیں بلکہ مثبت ہیں۔ پس جب کاشف تینوں ہیں تو بعض حنفیہ نے صرف قیاس ہی کو کیوں کا شف قرار دیا۔
قوله فمبنى على انه اصرح فى الفرعية فتأمل. اس عبارت سے بعض حنفیہ پر جو اعتراض وارد ہے اسکے جواب کی طرف اشارہ ہے۔
جواب:۔ یہ صحیح ہے کہ بعض حنفیہ سے منقول ہیکہ صرف قیاس کاشف و مظہر الا حکام ہے مگر ان کی یہ مراد ہر گز نہیں ہے کہ صرف قیاس ہی کاشف ہے اور سنت و اجماع کاشف نہیں ہیں۔ بلکہ وہ حضرات قیاس کے ساتھ ساتھ سنت و اجماع کو بھی خطاب شرعی کا کاشف و مظہر مانتے ہیں۔ البتہ صرف قیاس پر کاشف کا اس لئے اطلاق کرتے ہیں کہ سنت و اجماع کے بالمقابل خطاب شرعی کی فرعیت میں قیاس زیادہ صریح اور واضح ہے چونکہ قیاس میں استنباط حکم کے وقت ایک اصل (مقیس علیہ ) کی ضرورت پڑتی ہے تا کہ مقیس علیہ کے حکم کی علت کو سامنے رکھکر فرع و مقیس کی علت کے اشتراک کی تقدیر پر مقیس علیہ کے حکم کا مقیس میں تعدیہ کیا جاسکے ۔ اسکے برعکس سنت واجماع سے اخذ حکم کیلئے ان دونوں کے علاوہ مزید کسی امر آخر کی ضرورت نہیں پڑتی۔ اگر چہ یہ دونوں بھی خطاب از لی شرعی کیلئے کاشف ہیں اور اسکے فرع ہی ہیں مگر قیاس کے بہ نسبت ان دونوں کی فرعیت اخف واخفی ہے۔ چنانچہ ان دونوں کی فرعیت کے اخف واخفی ہونے کی وجہ سے ان علماء نے سنت و اجماع پر مظہر و کاشف ہونے کا اطلاق نہیں کیا اور صرف قیاس کو ہی کا شف کہا۔
His statement: 'And from the Hanafiyya that analogy is a manifestor contrary to the Sunnah and Ijma.' In the above statement, an objection is indicated. The objection: It is clear from your presentation that the Sunnah, Ijma, and analogy—all three principles—are revealers for the address. However, contrary to that, from the speech of some Hanafiyya it appears that for the address, only analogy is the revealer; as for the Sunnah and Ijma, they are not revealers but rather affirmers. So when all three are revealers, why did some Hanafiyya declare only analogy as revealer? His statement: 'It is based on the fact that it is more explicit in branch-hood, so reflect.' From this expression, an answer to the objection that occurred against some Hanafiyya is indicated. The answer: It is true that it is transmitted from some Hanafiyya that only analogy is the revealer and manifestor of rulings. But their intent is by no means that only analogy is the revealer and the Sunnah and Ijma are not revealers. Rather, those respected persons, along with analogy, consider the Sunnah and Ijma also as revealers and manifestors of the divine address. However, they apply the term 'revealer' only to analogy because, compared to the Sunnah and Ijma, in the branch-hood of the divine address, analogy is more explicit and clear. Since in analogy, at the time of deriving a ruling, there is need of an origin (the analogized-upon) so that, keeping in view the cause of the ruling of the analogized-upon, the ruling of the analogized-upon can be extended to the branch based on the presumed sharing of cause in the branch. In contrast, for deriving a ruling from the Sunnah and Ijma, there is no need for any other thing besides these two. Although these two are also revealers for the divine address and are branches of it, but compared to analogy, the branch-hood of these two is hidden and more hidden. Consequently, due to the hidden and more hidden nature of their branch-hood, those scholars did not apply the term 'manifestor and revealer' to the Sunnah and Ijma, and declared only analogy as the revealer.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about why some Hanafis consider qiyas the only 'kashif' (revealer/manifestor) of divine rulings, while sunnah and ijma are not given that label. The commentator first presents an objection: since all three (sunnah, ijma, qiyas) are means of deriving rulings, why single out qiyas? The response clarifies that it is a terminological preference, not a substantive denial. Qiyas is more clearly a branch (far') of the original ruling because it requires an analogical basis (asl) and a shared cause ('illa). Sunnah and ijma are also branches, but their derivation is more direct and less obviously dependent on an earlier source. Hence, some Hanafis reserve 'kashif' for qiyas to emphasize its derivative nature. The passage uses the terms 'muzhir' (manifestor) and 'kashif' (revealer) interchangeably, and the key point is that this is a matter of clarity in branch-hood (far'iyya), not a difference in the essential function of these sources.

Key Terms
كاشف (kāshif): That which reveals or makes manifest a ruling from the divine address (khitāb shar‘ī).
مظهر (muẓhir): Synonym of kashif; that which manifests a ruling from the divine address.
فرعية (far‘iyya): The quality of being a branch (far‘) derived from an original principle (asl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do some Hanafis call only qiyas a 'kashif' of divine rulings?
A) Because sunnah and ijma are not valid sources of law.
B) Because qiyas is more clearly a branch of the original address than sunnah and ijma.
C) Because qiyas was revealed later than sunnah and ijma.
D) Because sunnah and ijma are only for worship, not for rulings.

Key Takeaway
Some Hanafis call qiyas the only 'kashif' to emphasize its explicit derivative nature, but they fully affirm sunnah and ijma as equally valid revealers of divine rulings.

Real vs. Considerational Categories: Resolving Apparent Contradictions in Usul
Refuting objections about the impossibility of multiple categories applying to one entity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real (haqiqi) and considerational (i'tibari) categories, and apply this distinction to resolve objections about the coexistence of ijab and wujub in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Real categories cannot coexist; considerational categories can — the Hanafi key to resolving apparent contradictions.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, a recurring challenge is how to classify concepts like ijab (obligating) and wujub (obligation) without falling into logical contradictions. Critics argue that if these belong to different categories (action vs. passion), they cannot be unified in one entity. This passage presents the Hanafi response by introducing a crucial distinction between real categories (the ten true genera) and considerational categories (metaphorical labels). Understanding this distinction is essential for navigating usul disputes and defending Hanafi positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
Recognise that the dispute is about whether two mutually exclusive categories can apply to a single thing. The opponents argue from Ibn Sina that categories are by essence mutually exclusive.
The core is the logical principle of mutual exclusivity of real genera.
2
State the Opposing Objections
List the three objections: (1) from Ibn Sina's statement in al-Shifa', (2) the impossibility of two genera in one rank, (3) that essence and essential cannot differ by consideration alone.
These objections are raised by Mirza Jan and others.
3
Analyse the Hanafi Response: Distinction of Categories
The author clarifies that Sayyid meant considerational categories, not real ones. Real categories are indeed mutually exclusive, but considerational ones can coexist because they are metaphorical labels, not true genera.
This distinction is the key to the Hanafi defense.
4
Examine the Two Meanings of Action/Passion
Break down action and passion into two senses: (i) gradual effect/affection (genuine categories) and (ii) absolute effect/affection (not real categories). Ijab and wujub, in the context of inner speech, are only action/passion in the second, considerational sense.
This shows that no real contradiction arises.
5
Summarise the Practical Consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: if ijab and wujub are considerational, they can be unified without violating the rule of mutual exclusivity. This safeguards Hanafi classification of kalam nafsi as quality, not action/passion.
This resolves the objection and upholds the Hanafi position.
